CURRENCY EQUIVALENTS
Until recently, the Sri Lanka Rupee was pegged
to the Pound Sterling at a parity rate of X 1.00 = Rs 15.60.
On May 24, 1976, the Sri Lanka Rupee was officially linked
to a basket of currencies with the initial parity rate based
on the prevailing Rupee/Pound rate. Both the composition of
the basket and the weights assigned to the currencies in it
were revised on March 12, 1977. The current Rupee/US Dollar
rate has been used throughout the report, except where stated
to the contrary. 1/
US$1           =  Rs 7.28
Rs 1           =  US$0.137
Rs 1 million  =  US$137,363

= 50.8 kg                 =  112 lb
1 bushel (bu) of paddy        =  45 lb
1 pint                        =  0.57 liters
1 acre (ac)                   =  0.405 hectare (ha)
1 mile (mi)                   =  1.609 kilometers (km)
1 square mile (sq mi)         =  640 ac (259 ha)
1 foot (ft)                   =  30.5 centimeters (cm)
1/   Through the sale and purchase of Foreign Exchange
Entitlement Certificates (FEECs), Sri Lanka effec-
tively practices a dual exchange rate. Most non-
foodgrain imports have to pay a surcharge of 65%
through the purchase of FEECs. Most non-traditional
exports receive a 65% premium over the official rate
through the sale of FEECs.

SRI LANKA
APPRAISAL OF MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT II
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        Agriculture plays a major role in Sri Lanka's economy.  It
accounts for about one-third of the GNP, more than one-half of all employ-
ment, and about four-fifths of export earnings. Historically, the country
has depended upon export earnings from tree crops -- tea, rubber and coco-
nuts -- to pay for imports of foodgrains and other goods. However, agri-
cultural production has stagnated since 1970 and, with adverse changes in
the terms of trade, Sri Lanka has found it increasingly difficult to import
almost 50% of its foodgrain requirements. This has prompted a re-examination
of past policies and increased food self-sufficiency is now a major objec-
tive for the Government's agricultural policies. Two corner stones in the
Government's strategy to increase food production are better utilization of
existing irrigation facilities and creation of new irrigation potential.
ii.       The Mahaweli Ganga is Sri Lanka's largest river, draining an area
of about 4,000 sq mi. It is the principal source of water available for
irrigation in the dry zone, which covers the northern and eastern part of
the island. The river rises in the mountainous southern-central part of the
island, which receives an annual rainfall of 175 to 200 inches, and discharges
to the north-east into the Bay of Bengal. The richness of the water resources
at its source is contrasted with the relatively low rainfall (35 to 75 inches)
in the dry zone.
iii.      The present Master Plan for the Mahaweli Development Scheme, formu-
lated in 1968 by a UNDP/FAO team, envisages 900,000 ac of irrigation and 970 MW
of hydropower. Construction of Stage I of the scheme started with Bank Group
assistance in 1972 and is now almost completed. Stage I consists of a 40 MW
power station, and a transbasin diversion from the Mahaweli Ganga at Pogolla
to improve water supply to some 130,000 ac of existing irrigated land and to
provide adequate water for the irrigation of 90,000 ac of new land most of
which is located in the present project area. The Stage II works, which
would utilize water diverted by Stage I to irrigate 71,000 ac of new land,
were started in 1974. So far, irrigation infrastructure has been completed
for 6,100 ac and work is in progress on 24,600 ac. The proposed project
would complete the second stage of the Master Plan.
iv.       The project would cover a contiguous area of 106,000 ac.  It would
include: (a) construction of irrigation, drainage and road infrastructure
for 40,300 ac (US$13.7 M net of administration costs and contingencies);
(b) clearing of jungle and preparing 40,300 ac for cultivation as well as
settling some 15,300 families on 2-1/2 ac plots (US$18.2 M); (c) construction
and O&M facilities and equipment (US$12.6 M) for 71,000 ac; (d) production



- ii -
support ici the form of farm equipment, marketing, transport and processing
facilities for 71,000 ac (US$13.1 M); (e) improved agricultural extension
services for the whole 106,000 ac (US$1.9 M); (f) social infrastructure --
village wells, schools, medical and community development facilities -- for
64,900 ac (US$6.4 M); and (g) technical assistance and monitorlng (US$1.2 M).
v.        Total project costs are estimated at US$100.5 M equivalent, in-
cluding US$20.5 M in import taxes and duties. The foreign exchange com-
ponent is estimated at US$32.6 M, or 41% of the total net of taxes and
duties. Civil works and buildings amount to US$37.8 M; equipment and
vehicles, US$26.3 M; technical assistance, US$0.6 M; engineering, settle-
ment assistance, administration and monitoring, US$6.5 M; physical con-
tingencies, US$6.8 M and price contingencies, US$22.2 M.
vi.       With assistance from the Bank Group, GOSL has tentatively secured
credits of about US$6.0 M equivalent from Canada, US$5.0 M from The Nether-
lands, US$7.2 M from UK, and US$5.0 M from the US, for financing parts of the
project costs. Together with the proposed IDA credit of US$19.0 M, they would
finance all foreign exchange costs and about 20% of local costs, or 53% of the
total project cost net of taxes and duties.
vii.      Project implementation would take about five years.  The Mahaweli
Development Board (MDB), an agency under the Ministry of Irrigation, Power
and Highways, would be responsible for implementation of all civil works and
buildings proposed under the project. The Department of Agriculture in the
Ministry of Agriculture and Lands would be responsible for reorganizing
and strengthening the extension service. The Sri Lanka Tractor Corporation
would be responsible for the procurement and distribution of farm equipment
to the project area farmers as well as for procurement and distribution of
construction and O&M equipment. Interagency coordination of planning and im-
plementation would be provided by the Mahaweli Coordination Committee, chaired
by the Secretary of Irrigation, at the policy level and by technical sub-
committees at the working level.  After completion of construction, all build-
ings would be handed over to the agencies concerned. The MDB, initially, would
be responsible for operation and maintenance of the irrigation infrastructure.
At the field level, interagency coordination would be provided by the Project
Advisory Committee, chaired by MDB's Resident Project Manager which would in-
clude officers from the concerned agencies as well as farmer representatives.
viii.     Equipment and vehicles to be financed under the credits from Canada,
UK and the US would be procured in accordance with their respective procurement
guidelines. Other equiDment and vehicles would be procured through interna-
tional competitive bidding in accordance with IDA guidelines.  Since the civil
works and buildings (US$37.8 M) would be relatively small, labor-intensive,
scattered over a wide area and implemented seasonally over a five year period,
international competitive bidding woiuld not be practical. Most of these works
(US$28.7 M) would be carried out after local competitive bidding or through
small unit price contracts. About 24% of all works would be carried out by
MDB under force account (US$0.6 M) or by public agencies under negotiated
contracts (US$8.5 M).



- iii -
ix.       The irrigation infrastructure provided under the project would
directly benefit some 15,300 families who, at present, are landless or
practice subsistence agriculture on small holdings. Most of them would
be given secure tenure for the first time and their incomes, presently
one-half of the national average, would reach the national level. The
expansion of the cropped area and the more intensive cultivation, made
possible by the provision of irrigation, would generate an annual farm
employment of three million man-days or about 12,000 full time jobs. The
project would have substantial secondary employment effects in marketing,
processing, supply of farm inputs and services, and would help relieve
the large unemployment and underemployment prevailing in and around the
project area.
x.        At full development in 1986, the project would increase the
production of: foodgrains by 42,300 tons; chillies, 1,100 tons; oilseeds,
3,800 tons; and vegetables, 3,600 tons. At projected world market prices,
this would represent a net annual foreign exchange saving of about US$8.0 M.
The project's economic rate of return would be 21%. Sensitivity tests
indicate that the project would remain economically viable even under a
combination of unlikely adverse circumstances.
xi.       The proposed project would constitute the first major integrated
development of unirrigated land under the Mahaweli Master Plan. In its
approach to project management, community participation and supporting
services, the project was formulated with an overriding concern for replic-
ability in future land development in the dry zone.
xii.      Subject to appropriate assurances to be obtained from the Govern-
ment, the proposed project is suitable for an IDA credit of US$19 million.
The Borrower would be the Republic of Sri Lanka.






SRI LANKA
APPRAISAL OF MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT II
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of Sri Lanka (GOSL) has requested IDA assistance
for a part of the Mahaweli Development Scheme. The scheme, which is based
on a Master Plan prepared by a UNDP/FAO team in 1968, envisages the irrigation
development of 900,000 ac and 970 MW of hydropower. Stage I of the scheme,
supported by the Bank Group through Credit 174-CE (US$14.5 M) and Loan 653-CE
(US$14.5), was started in 1972 and is expected to be completed in 1977. It
consists of a 40 14W hydropower plant and a transbasin diversion from Mahaweli
Ganga at Polgolla to the present project area and partial rehabilitation of
an existing irrigation system serving some 130,000 ac. The Stage II works,
started in 1974, would utilize part of the water diverted by Stage I to
irrigate 71,000 ac of new land in north central Sri Lanka in order to settle
27,000 families. The proposed project would complete Stage II and provide
agricultural extension services to the 71,000 ac as well as 35,000 ac of
adjacent irrigated land, at an estimated cost of US$100.5 M (US$80.0 M net
of taxes and duties).
1.02      This would be the Bank Group's fifth operation in support of the
country's efforts in water resources development. In addition to supporting
the Mahaweli Ganga Stage I Project, the Bank Group has been involved in a
Lift Irrigation Project (Credit 121-CE, US$2.0 M), a Drainage and Reclamation
Project (Credit 168-CE, US$2.5 M), and a Tank Irrigation Modernization Project
(Credit 666-CE, US$5.0 M).
1.03      The feasibility study for Stage II, provided for under the Loan/
Credit Agreements for Stage I, was prepared by the consulting firm SOGREAH
of France. An FAO/IBRD Cooperative Program Mission visited Sri Lanka in
early 1975 to finalize a preparation report (Report No. 17/75 CEY 7), and
the project was appraised in April/May 1975 by a Bank Group Mission. In
February 1976, GOSL requested that the project be reformulated. A followup
Mission, comprising: Messrs. P. Ljung; S. Baker; C. J. R. Bridge, and Ms.
M. Nguyen (IDA), and Mr. C. D. Datey (Consultant), visited Sri Lanka in May/
June 1976 for this purpose. This report is based on the findings of the
followup Mission. At various stages, the IDA Missions were accompanied by
representatives of four prospective co-financing countries: Messrs. R. Bailey
and E. Yendall (Canada); R. Glazener (The Netherlands); W. Franklin (UK); and
C. Billings, J. Coles, R. Perry and J. Watson (USA).



- 2 -
II. BACKGROUND
General
2.01      Sri Lanka has an estimated population of about 13.8 million, 80% of
which is rural. Of the total land area of 16.2 million ac (about 25,300 sq
mi), about 5 million ac are used for agriculture. The climate is characterized
by nearly constant temperatures and large variations in rainfall (Annex 1).
The island can be divided into two distinct precipitation zones: the wet
zone (average annual rainfall over 75 inches), situated in the southwest
quadrant and covering about 30% of the land area, and the dry zone (average
annual rainfall ranging from 35 to 75 inches) covering the remainder of the
island. The wet zone supports more than three-quarters of the total popula-
tion and accounts for about 70% of the cultivated land.
The Economy
2.02      The average per capita income is low (US$150 in 1975).  Government
policies have resulted in a well-developed system of health care and other
public services, a high literacy rate, and an absence of extreme poverty.
These developments, however, have not been accompanied by a satisfactory
growth in output, and, since 1970, the annual increase in gross national pro-
duct (GNP) has averaged 2.5%, somewhat higher than the population growth rate
of 1.7%. The slow pace of economic development is mainly due to inadequate
investments in the productive sectors. Due to the poor production perfor-
mance, the employment situation has deteriorated and about one-third of the
labor force is unemployed or severely underemployed. During recent years,
prices of major imports, foodgrains, fertilizers and petroleum products have
risen sharply while price increases for the main exports, tea, coconuts and
rubber, have been relatively moderate, and the country now faces a severe
foreign exchange shortage. The budgetary resources of the Government are
barely adequate to cover its current expenditures, leaving most capital
formation to the vagaries of external assistance.
Agricultural and Irrigation
2.03      Agriculture contributes about one-third of the GNP, more than one-
half of all employment, and over four-fifths of export earnings. About half
of the total cultivated area of 5.0 M ac is under tree crops, primarily tea,
rubber and coconuts. The other half is under field crops, principally paddy
(1.8 M ac), manioc (0.3 M ac) and chillies, maize and pulses (about 0.1 M ac
each). The cropping intensity on paddy lands ranges from about 185% in the
wet zone to about 110% in the dry zone. The average for the country is about
150%.
2.04      In the decade ending in 1973, agricultural output increased at an
annual rate of 2.3%. Production in subsequent years has been adversely
affected by droughts. While foodgrain production increased 3.1% annually,
output in the tree crop sector increased 1.2% annually. The poor performance
of the tree crop sector is largely a result of inadequate investments due to
low financial returns and an uncertain investment climate. Production of field



-3-
crops has suffered from poor water management, scarcity of farm inputs, lack
of adequate farm power for tillage, poor extension services, marketing and
credit facilities and from a system of controls and subsidies which reduced
farmer incentive. Measures taken during the past few years to increase field
crop output have included: increases in guaranteed price levels; lifting of
controls on movement of paddy; banning imports of subsidiary crops such as
chillies, onions, potatoes and pulses; establishment of Agricultural Pro-
ductivity Committees (APCs) to improve the supply of farm inputs and support-
ing services, and most importantly, an increased emphasis on efficient water
utilization and management.
2.05      Irrigation in Sri Lanka dates back to about 600 B.C., when a series
of village tanks for local rainwater storage were constructed in the north.
Over the last century, many of these tanks have been reconditioned and incor-
porated into new irrigation systems. Currently, about 900,000 ac, throughout
the island, are irrigated and efforts are continuing to expand the area. In
addition, the Government has undertaken a number of multipurpose water develop-
ment projects, including the Gal Oya, Uda Walowe and Mahaweli Ganga.
The Mahaweli Development Scheme
2.06      The Mahaweli Ganga is Sri Lanka's largest river, draining an area
of about 4,000 sq mi. It rises in the mountainous south-central part of the
island, which receives an annual rainfall of 175-200 inches, and discharges
an average of about 7 M ac-ft annually into the Bay of Bengal. It is the
principal source of water for irrigation in the dry zone. The Master Plan for
Mahaweli Development, formulated by a UNDP/FAO team in 1968 envisages: provi-
sion of irrigation to about 650,000 ac of undeveloped land; improvement in the
water supply to and partial rehabilitation of about 250,000 ac of existing
irrigated land; and development of about 510 MW of hydropower from multipur-
pose reservoirs. It would also create the potential for a further development
of about 460 MW of hydropower in single purpose reservoirs. As a result of
the great importance attached to increased food production, the emphasis in
the present implementation program is on irrigation, leaving much of the power
development to later stages.
2.07      Construction of Stage I of the Master Plan began with Bank Group
assistance in 1972 and is now nearly completed. It consists of a transbasin
diversion from the Mahaweli Ganga at Polgolla to the present project area,
provision for an assured water supply to some 130,000 ac of existing irrigated
lands and a 40 MW power station. Stage II works, which would utilize water di-
verted by Stage I to provide irrigation to 71,000 ac of new land, were started
in 1974. So far, irrigation infrastructure has been completed for 6,100 ac
and work is in progress on 24,600 ac. The proposed project would complete
Stage II, and provide agricultural extension services to the 71,000 ac as
well as to 35,000 ac of adjacent irrigated lands.
Settlement Schemes
2.08      Since the turn of the century, many settlement schemes have been
introduced to relieve overpopulation and unemployment in the more densely



- 4 -
populated areas of the wet zone. During the late 1950s and early 1960s,
settlers were admitted to the schemes prior to land development and were left
to do this work themselves. This resulted in a slow build up of benefits, a
wasteful use of water and a high drop-out rate. On the recommendation of an
FAO/IBRD Mission in 1966, some major colonization schemes were reorganized as
"Special Projects" under Resident Project Managers in order to increase agri-
cultural productivity and utilize irrigation water more efficiently. The
success of these projects, resulted in the extension of the "Special Projects"
program in 1969. However, the expansion has been limited due to the shortage
of qualified and experienced managers.
2.09      In recent years, the Government has also experimented with collec-
tive farming and self management in low cost programs such as Youth Settle-
ment Schemes, Cooperative Farms and District Development Council Schemes. An
assessment of all these schemes, made by the Agrarian Research and Training
Institute (ARTI) in 1974, showed that few of them have been successful in
achieving the desired objectives due to inadequate supporting services, in-
appropriate settler selection, lack of competent management and insecure
tenure.
Rural Institutions and Legal Framework
2.10      During the early 1970s, rural institutions underwent a drastic
change. A number of laws were enacted to increase agricultural productivity
and to achieve a more equitable income distribution. The Agricultural Pro-
ductivity Law (APL) of 1972 and the Agricultural Lands Law (ALL) of 1973
established the Agricultural Productivity Committees (APCs) and reorganized
the Cultivation Committees (CCs). The APL obliges all owners and occupiers
of agricultural land to use it to the maximum benefit of the country and
provides for dispossession from owners or tenants who do not properly utilize
their land. The ALL regulates tenants' rights and provides for security of
tenure and prescribes rents payable to landlords.
2.11      The APCs have the responsibility to promote, coordinate and develop
agriculture in the areas they cover (typically 10,000 ac) and to assist the
Ministry of Agriculture and Lands (MAL) in formulating and implementing annual
programs for crop and livestock production. They are directly responsible,
through the CCs (each covering about 1,000 ac), for the water distribution
below the 50 ac level. Because of their power to regulate cropping patterns,
the APCs also have a responsibility for the overall water allocation within
their areas. The APCs and the CCs, whose members are appointed by the MAL,
do not have any professional staff. They work through, in cooperation with,
and to a large extent under the supervision of the Government's technical
staff. The most important task of an APC is the preparation of the Annual
Production Program, which is done in consultation with the Government's agri-
cultural officers. The program is then subject to the approval of MAL and
the District Government Agent. In this process the APC represents the far-
mers' interests, while the Government staff provides the technical input and
assures the feasibility of the program.



- 5 -
III. THE PROJECT AREA
General
3.01      The project area is located in Anuradhapura, Kurunegala and Matale
Districts in north-central Sri Lanka. The proposed project would cover a con-
tiguous area of 106,000 ac; comprising 35,000 ac of old irrigated land, 30,700
ac presently under development and 40,300 ac of undeveloped or new land (Map
11908R and Annex 1, para 2). The estimated population in the project area is
about 140,000, with an average family size of 5.5 persons. All project com-
ponents would be provided to the main project area of 40,300 ac. The other
areas would benefit only from selected project components.
Climate (Annex 1)
3.02      Climate is tropical with mean monthly temperatures ranging from
76 F°to 83 F, making it suitable for year round cropping. Mean annual rain-
fall is about 56 inches and almost two-thirds of that occurs during the maha
season (October to January). The unpredictability of the rainfall makes irri-
gation necessary for high and stable yields in the maha season. Cultivation
during the yala season (March to June) depends almost entirely on irrigation.
Topography and Soils
3.03      The project area is characterized by gently undulating topography
with slopes ranging from 1% to 5%, with an average of about 1.5% to 2% over
the greater part of the area. Two soil types predominate: (i) reddish brown,
moderately coarse, highly permeable soils, located on uplands with rolling
topography and (ii) brown to grey-brown, finer and poorly drained bottom land
soils with higher silt and clay content. All the soils are slightly acidic.
The reddish brown soils that occupy about 60% of the area are best suited
for the production of upland crops, while the bottom soils (about 40% of the
area) are ideal for paddy production.
Socio-Economic Conditions
3.04      The old irrigated areas consist primarily of settlement schemes
established between 1946 and 1964, with a population of approximately 70,000.
The farm sizes range from 2.0 to 5.0 ac, with an average of about 3.0 ac. In
the areas presently under development, irrigation infrastructure was completed
for 6,100 ac and about 2,300 families were settled during 1976. Irrigation
works are in progress on the remaining 24,600 ac. The existing population in
these areas is about 33,000. In the 6,100 ac area that has already been
settled, the farm size is uniformly 2.5 ac of farm land and 0.5 ac of home-
stead. In the remaining areas, the present situation is very similar to that
in the main project area.



-6-
3.05      The main project area is sparsely populated with an estimated pop-
ulation of about 37,000. Most of the settlements are purana (old) villages
with a traditional pattern of clustered homesteads next to a small village
tank. In recent years, a considerable number of migrants have moved into the
area and begun cultivating public land. The main occupation is subsistance
farming, with about 20% of the labor force permanently engaged outside agri-
culture. Farm incomes are low and only about one-third of the families derive
all their income from the family farm. Most of the purana families cultivate
both paddy (1.5 ac) and chena --rotational slash and burn--(0.5 ac) and have
a fairly large homestead lot (1.0 ac) where they grow tree crops, chillies and
vegetables. The more recently settled families in the area tend to cultivate
homestead (1.0 ac) and chena (1.6 ac) land only.
3.06      The land tenure situation in the purana villages is complicated.
Most of the tank irrigated paddy lands with traditional ownership rights have
been inherited and subdivided from one generation to another, which has re-
sulted in small and fragmented holdings. A small number of families lease
their paddy land from the Government at a nominal rent. This land can be
inherited but not sold, mortgaged, or fragmented. About 80% of the paddy
land is cultivated by the owner. The remainder is rented under a variety of
arrangements such as share cropping, cash lease or fixed rent paid in kind.
The rent varies substantially from a fixed 6 bu per ac in some cases to half
the produce in about 50% of the cases. About three-quarters of all the land
-- almost exclusively upland area -- is owned by the Government. The peasants
can farm this land with annual permits. However, in most cases, cultivation
is practiced without permit.
Land Use and Farming Practices
3.07      On the old irrigated lands paddy is virtually the only crop grown
in the maha season (yield: 1.4 tons/ac) and it accounts for about 90% of
the cultivated area in the yala season (yield: 1.2 tons/ac). The cropping
intensity is about 150%, with 80% to 90% in maha and 60% to 70% in yala. The
areas where construction is underway are in transition, their pre-development
(i.e. pre-1974) situation was similar to that existing in the main project
area. Most of the main project area is covered by jungle, varying from dense
to light scrub. Irrigated, low yield paddy (0.8 tons/ac in maha and 0.7 tons/
ac in yala) is grown mostly on lowlands around village tanks (about 5,000 ac
in maha and 1,000 ac in yala, see Annex 11). Sowing of maha crops starts in
October/ November, after the tanks have been filled to a level deemed adequate
to ensure the water supply for the crop. Because of inadequate farm power for
land preparation and the priority given to chena cultivation, the sowing is
extended into late January. The crop is harvested between February and April.
Subject to water availability in the tanks, yala paddy is broadcast during
March/April for harvest in June/ July. Broadcasting is preferred to trans-
planting, but row seeding, particularly in the yala season, is becoming more
popular as it allows easier weed control.



3.08      Cereals (maize, sorghum and finger millet), pulses (cow peas,
green and black grams), and chillies are grown under rainfed conditions on
the non-irrigated highlands and under chena cultivation in the maha season.
Sesamum is grown in the yala season. The area cultivated under rainfed
conditions is about 8,000 ac. Coconuts, bananas, and vegetables are grown
on the homestead lots.
Agricultural Supporting Services (Annex 4)
3.09      Marketing and Processing.  The PMB has a legal monopoly over paddy
purchase with the cooperatives as sole agents. However, with the recent
removal of legal restrictions on the movement of paddy, the activities of
private traders have increased and they have purchased a large part of the
1975 crop. The milling is done primarily by private mills under contracts
with PMB. It is expected, however, that PMB would continue to play an import-
ant role in paddy marketing and milling. Existing milling and storage facil-
ities are adequate for present demand. Other crops (chillies, cereals and
pulses) are mostly marketed through private channels.
3.10      Farm Equipment.  About two-thirds of land preparation is done by
bullocks and the remainder by four-wheel and, to some extent, two-wheel trac-
tors. The four-wheel tractors are usually owned by licensed tractor operators
who move over a large part of northern Sri Lanka in pace with the varied plow-
ing season. Two-wheel tractors are owned primarily by well-to-do farmers.
3.11      Agricultural Extension and Research.  Extension suffers from a
shortage of vehicles, equipment and staff quarters as well as the absence of
a well-defined, goal-oriented work program. Following IDA recommendations,
the project area (106,000 ac) was recently organized into a separate extension
district under a Project Agricultural Extension Officer (PAEO). Additional
staff is being recruited. A regional training center designed to train staff
and contact farmers is located in the project area at Maha Illuppallama, where
research on crop and cropping systems for the dry zone is concentrated. Rice
breeding is conducted at the nearby Batalagoda station, which has produced a
number of good modern varieties. Research findings have been successfully
applied to two irrigated pilot schemes within the project area.
3.12      Cash Inputs.  Seeds are distributed by the extension service and
varieties adaptable to all climatic zones and seasons are generally avail-
able. Fertilizers are primarily distributed through the branch cooperatives
in the villages and agro-chemicals principally through the private sector.
However, some APCs have also started to sell these inputs. The supply of
fertilizer is usually sufficient to meet the demand, but deliveries are not
always timely. Pesticides and herbicides are generally available but there
is a shortage of application equipment. Institutional credit is provided
through the Bank of Ceylon and the People's Bank. However, about 85% of the
short- and medium-term credit is provided by private sources. Due to inade-
quately trained field staff, lack of supervision and poor credit discipline,
the recovery rates on institutional credit are, generally, low (about 50%).



-8-
Transport
3.13      As in most parts of Sri Lanka, the project area has a good trans-
portation network. The main railroads from Colombo to Jaffna and Trincomalee
pass through the project area. The area is served by good all-weather
asphalted roads. The secondary roads are usually graded and many are
gravelled. However, the village and farm roads are often nothing more than
tracks and are usually impassable during the wet season. The area is served
by regular bus service.
Social Services (Annex 5)
3.14      Education and health care in Sri Lanka are generally considered
good compared with other countries in South Asia and the existing population
in the project area is well served in these respects. Primary schools are
usually within easy walking distance. Health services are provided by a
network of medical institutions. Other public services are also adequate for
the needs of the present population.
IV. THE PROJECT
Project Concept
4.01      The proposed project would complete the first major integrated dev-
elopment of new lands under the Mahaweli Master Plan. It builds on experiences
gained from both the on-going Mahaweli works and previous settlement schemes
in Sri Lanka (paras 2.08-2.09). The project is of a pattern that can be repli-
cated in the future. Primary considerations in project design have been:
(a) Optimal use of the available water resources to increase
food production and to maximize farm incomes. This would
be achieved through the provision of irrigation and drain-
age facilities for each farm with adequate control struc-
tures, lining of critical canal sections and proper land
preparation.
(b) Equitable distribution of project benefits through settle-
ment on uniform 2.5 ac farms.
(c) Development of a spirit of self-reliance among settlers.
This would be achieved by letting the farmers do most of
the on-farm works in return for payments from MDB. The land
would be sold to the settlers, with secure tenure, at a
price which, in nominal terms, would recover about 60% of
the cost for irrigation and land development works.
(d) Provision of well organized, highly intensive extension
services in order to train the settlers in new and improved
farming techniques.



(e) Creation of settlements that are socially and economically
viable through the provision of basic social infrastructure
and agricutural supporting services. Especially in the
selection of settlers, preference would be given to the
population living in and around the project area to retain
existing social links and to maintain a culturally homogeneous
population. Following the traditional village pattern, the
settlements would be grouped into small neighborhood units
(hamlets) each with a population of about 100 families.
(f) Strong coordination at the project level would be achieved
through the appointment of a Resident Project Manager (RPM)
assisted by Deputy RPMs for Agriculture, Community Development
and Engineering. Community participation in planning and
implementation would be supported through a close coordina-
tion between farmers' organizations and project officers.
Project Components
4.02      Since the development throughout the project area is not uniform,
the various components of the project would cover different areas as shown in
the table below. The table also shows the number of families benefitted and
the investment cost per family for each project component.

Irrigation and Land Development (Annex 3)
4.03      Irrigation Infrastructure.  The first 18 mi of the new 28 mi Kalawewa
RB Main Canal are under construction and the remaining 10 mi would be con-
structed under the project. Four large tanks along the canal route would be
improved and would serve as equalizing reservoirs to reduce operational losses
along the canal. Eight branch canals, 31 mi in total length, would take off
from the main canal to serve the project area. About 250 mi of distributary



- 11 -
canals and 400 mi of field channels would convey irrigation water to the irri-
gation turnouts.   Operation and maintenance roadways would be provided along
one embankment of the main, branch and distributary canals. About 10 mi of
natural main drains would be improved and 100 mi of secondary and 110 mi of
tertiary drains constructed. All canals would have structures necessary to
properly control and measure irrigation deliveries throughout the project area,
giving the system considerable flexibility and high operational efficiency.
4.04      On-farm Development Works.  The agricultural and settlement areas
would be cleared of trees and brush and cross ripped by heavy tractors. The
MDB would plow all areas when the settlers arrive to assist them in leveling
work and in construction of soil conservation bunds.- About 1,500 mi of farm
ditches and drains would provide water distribution and drainage to each 2.5
ac farm within the turnout area.
4.05      Construction and O&M Equipment and Facilities.  Because of the
general shortage of heavy construction equipment and vehicles in Sri Lanka,
construction equipment and vehicles would be provided to complete the civil
works in accordance with the project implementation schedule. Equipment would
also be provided for a construction workshop to be set up in the project area.
To ensure that the irrigation works would be properly operated and maintained,
new equipment and O&M facilities (workshop, offices and staff quarters) would
be provided for this purpose.
Production Support (Annex 4)
4.06      Agricultural Extension and Research.  Agricultural extension serv-
ices would be reorganized and strengthened under the project. The number of
field extension workers (KVSs) would increase from about one per 500 farm
families to one per 250 families. This intensity would permit scheduled
weekly visits by the KVS to small farmer groups, along the lines successfully
adopted in a number of Bank Group financed projects in India. The project
would provide vehicles, staff quarters and demonstration equipment. The
Regional Agricultural Training Center at Maha Illuppallama, designed to train
extension staff and farmers, would be provided with additional equipment,
teaching aids, staff quarters and dormitory accommodations. A new Irrigation
and Water Management Pilot Project, jointly sponsored by MDB and the Irriga-
tion and Agriculture Departments, would be set up in the project area to in-
vestigate water management and cropping patterns. The scheme would also be
used as a demonstration area for extension workers and contact farmers.
4.07      Farm Equipment.  Since there is a severe shortage of farm power in
the project area -- both animals and tractors -- 300 four-wheel and 1,200 two-
wheel tractors with farm implements would be provided under the project. In
addition, 50 power sprayers and 1,300 hand sprayers would be provided for
plant protection.
4.08      Storage, Marketing and Processing.  Existing facilities in the pro-
ject area would be inadequate for handling the increased agricultural produc-
tion generated by the project. The following facilities would be provided:



- 12 -
(i) storage facilities for fertilizer, seed and paddy in each of the 50 vil-
lage centers; (ii) buildings for three new primary multipurpose cooperatives
and 24 new branch cooperatives and funds for the renovation of 26 existing
branch buildings. Vehicles (for transport of inputs and paddy) and some minor
equipment for the three primary cooperatives in the area; and (iii) buildings,
plant and equipment for a PMB processing complex (with a milling capacity of
1,000 tons per month and 5,000 tons of storage), 50 rice hullers and 25
thresher/winnowers for the Cultivation Committees in the project area.
4.09.     Market Roads.  To allow all-weather access throughout the project
area, some 50 mi of existing link roads would be improved and 25 mi of new
link roads would be constructed.  The 310 mi of O&M roads along the canals
would also be used by the farmers for transport.
Social Infrastructure (Annex 5)
4.10      Existing social facilities are adequate for the present population,
but, additional facilities would be essential for the 14,000 new families to
be settled in the area. The project would provide: (i) Schools -- about 600
classrooms in primary schools (grades one to nine) and 20 classrooms in
secondary schools (grades 10-11) with laboratories, playgrounds and staff
quarters. (ii) Medical Facilities -- two rural hospitals and two central dis-
pensaries and maternity wards together with vehicles, equipment and staff
quarters; (iii) Community Development Facilities -- about 50 small community
halls for village meetings, training sessions and recreation; and vehicles
and housing for community development staff; (iv) Village Wells -- a total of
1,235 wells for domestic use -- about one per 20 families; and (v) Roads in
New Villages -- some 200 mi of 15 ft-wide graded and unmetalled roads inside
the new hamlets and villages.
Other Project Components
4.11      Settlement (Annex 10).  Each settler would receive materials for a
temporary hut, seeds and planting material for his first crops, field tools
and a subsistence allowance of Rs 50 per month for five months. This assist-
ance would amount to about Rs 680 per family. In addition, the new settlers
would receive, for a period of one year, free food rations from the World
Food Program (WFP). The GOSL expects that the present agreement between GOSL
and WFP will be extended beyond the expiration date of March 1977. Equipment
and vehicles would be provided to the settlement staff.
4.12      Technical Assistance.  The project would provide the services of an
implementation advisor and an on-farm development specialist for a period of
three years each (Annex 9). An assurance was obtained that these two spe-
cialists would be appointed by January 1, 1978.
4.13      Monitoring Project Performance.  The project would include a program
to monitor irrigation efficiencies and to assess the agricultural, economic
and social impact of the project (Annex 9, Appendix 2). Funds would be pro-
vided for:  installation of about 50 flow recording stations; vehicles and
equipment; the service of one hydrologist for 12 months; and agro-economic
surveys by the Agrarian Research and Training Institute (ARTI).



- 13 -
Implementation Schedule (Annex 9, Figure 3)
4.14      Construction work in the project area is ongoing and under MDB's
present rate of implementation irrigation infrastructure and settlement would
be completed within three years; social infrastructure, within four years; and
on-farm works, within five years. It is expected that the project would be
fully implemented by June 1982. In order to ensure a well-planned settlement
process, an assurance was obtained that no settlers would be brought into an
area earlier than three months before the expected delivery of water to the
area.
Engineering
4.15      Field surveys have been completed and detailed maps for the layout
of the irrigation distribution and drainage systems are scheduled for comple-
tion during 1977. For farm layout and design of the irrigation and drainage
system within turnout areas, surveys would be made after clearing of forested
areas. Detailed design of major irrigation works were started by MDB in
January 1976 and would be completed during 1977. Design of works below the
field turnouts, each of which commands about 50 ac, would be done by the proj-
ect engineering office. All designs are in accordance with sound engineering
standards.
Water Supply, Demand and Quality (Annex 2)
4.16      Supply.  The water supply would come from:  (i) irrigation diver-
sions from the Mahaweli Ganga, which have priority over power releases through
facilities provided by Stage I of the Mahaweli Development Scheme; (ii) local
runoff collected by the major tanks within the project area, and (iii) return
flow from irrigation in the upper reaches of the project. About 60% of the
demand would be met by Mahaweli diversions, 25% by local yield and the remain-
der by return flow. An average of about 7.5 ft of water would be available
annually for irrigation over the entire project area.
4.17      Demand.  Water requirements, estimated by the Modified Penman method,
were based on a cropping pattern planned for maximum use of rainfall, as shown
in Annex 11, Figure 1. The irrigation demands for the Kandalama and Kalawewa
command areas would average about 765 MCM (620,000 ac-ft) annually. This would
amount to about 7.0 ft/yr for the gross service area (88,500 ac). The full
irrigation requirements would be supplied in about eight years out of ten and
irrigation shortages in the water deficient years would range from about 5% to
15%. Irrigation deliveries would be rotated between farms and between farm
channels and the smaller distributary canals, in accordance with irrigation
schedules developed during each crop season. This would permit the farmers to
irrigate more effectively than would be possible under continuous flow irriga-
tion practices.
4.18      Quality.  The waters of the Mahaweli Ganga and Amban Ganga are of
excellent quality for irrigation. Tanks within the project area have water
of slightly lower but satisfactory quality for irrigation.



- 14 -
Environmental Effects
4.19      Schistosomiasis does not occur in Sri Lanka and the principal water-
related disease in the area is malaria. The increased water storage in major
tanks, as a result of the project would be more than compensated by a substan-
tial reduction in the number of small village tanks, which are a more suitable
habitat for mosquitoes. The improvement of natural drains would remove tail-
water from the fields which stagnates in borrow areas and low spots. As a
precautionary measure, an assurance was obtained from GOSL that it would
institute a malaria monitoring program, and if necessary, take appropriate
preventive and remedial measures in the project area. The extension of the
irrigation season into the dry season would improve the recharge of village
wells and thus improve the domestic water supply. The net effect of the
project on public health and environment would be favorable.
V. COST ESTIMATES AND FINANCIAL ARRANGEMENTS
Project Cost Estimates
5.01      Total project costs are estimated at US$100.5 M equivalent, including
about US$20.5 M in taxes and duties. The foreign exchange component would be
about US$32.6 M or 41% of the total, net of taxes and duties. Costs estimates
are based on contracts recently awarded for similar ongoing works in the proj-
ect area and have been adjusted to the January 1977 level. Unit prices for
equipment and materials are based on January 1977 price quotations of suppliers.
Based on past experience, costs for administration, design and construction
supervision are estimated to be about 10% of construction costs. Physical con-
tingencies were estimated at: (i) 20% for irrigation infrastructure, reflect-
ing the lack of detailed mapping and field location of the distribution system
at the time of estimate; (ii) 15% for market roads; and (iii) 10% for social
infrastructure, stores and buildings as well as the monitoring program since
these requirements are fairly well defined. Price contingencies are estimated
at about 31% of the base cost estimate including taxes and duties in accord-
ance with the following anticipated annual inflation rates:
1977-1979     1980-81
Civil Works                      12%           10%
Equipment and Services            8%            7%

5.03      The above bilateral assistance combined with the proposed IDA con-
tribution of US$19.0 M would finance the full foreign exchange costs and
about 20% of local costs, or about 53% of the total project cost net of taxes
and duties. Commercial Banks and the buyers of farm equipment would provide
about US$9.7 M for financing the farm equipment. The Government would con-
tribute the remaining US$28.1 M. A condition of credit effectiveness would
be the prior or simultaneous effectiveness of agreements with the co-financiers.
An assurance was obtained that GOSL would provide adequate funds to the
agencies concerned for project implementation and that it would submit to IDA,
no later than November 1 each year, a detailed implementation and financial
plan for the following fiscal year.
Procurement
5.04      Equipment.  The estimated cost of equipment and vehicles (Annex 6)
is US$26.3 M including import taxes and duties (US$8.6 M) local handling costs
US$2.4 M and locally procured equipment (US$0.2 M). Most of the equipment and
vehicles would be furnished by the co-financiers, as indicated on the equipment
lists, in accordance with their procurement guidelines. The remainder would
be procured through international competitive bidding (ICB) in accordance with
Bank Group guidelines. A 15% preference margin, or prevailing customs duty
if lower, would be extended to local manufacturers in evaluation of bids.
Small off-the-shelf items costing less than US$10,000 for each order, and for
which international tendering is not practical for international tendering,
would be purchased through GOSL standard procedures which are satisfactory to
IDA. Such purchases, however, would be limited to an aggregate total of
US$250,000.



- 17 -
5.05      Civil Works.  Because most civil works would be labor-intensive,
relatively small, restricted to seasonal construction, scattered over a wide
area and could not be grouped into large contracts, they would be unsuitable
for ICB. The private sector in Sri Lanka has very limited capacity for civil
works construction. On the other hand, a number of public agencies have sub-
stantial experience in this type of work. Therefore, part of the works would
be awarded to private contractors after local competitive bidding (LCB) and
the remainder to public agencies under negotiated contracts.
5.06      An agreement has been reached that the award of contracts to public
agencies would be governed by the following conditions: (i) unit prices would
not exceed those in the lowest evaluated LCB bid for similar work; (ii) all
contractors would meet prequalification standards acceptable to IDA; (iii)
IDA would approve all public sector contracts before they are awarded, with
the exception of contracts below US$50,000 up to an aggregate of US$500,000
and (iv) the role of the Department of Machinery and Equipment (DME) would
be restricted to procurement, renting, repair and maintenance of equipment.
5.07      The following categories have been established for the construction
of the various project works: 1/
(a) Kalawewa Main Canal, Branch Canals and Village Tanks
(US$3.7 M). In view of the limited capacity of the
private sector, about 50% of these works would be
awarded to private contractors under LCB and 50%
would be awarded to public agencies under negotia-
ted contracts. The LCB contracts would be advertised
locally in accordance with standard Government pro-
cedures, which are satisfactory to IDA;
(b) Distributary Canals, Drainage Channels and On-Farm Develop-
ment (US$13.8 M). These works would be carried out following
LCB or would be let on a unit price basis to small village
contractors;
(c) Land Clearing and Preparation (US$11.2 M). The initial
clearing of brush and trees up to 6 ft girth would be
awarded on a unit area basis following LCB or by unit
price contracts established at the beginning of each
construction season following LCB. Works requiring
heavy equipment would be allocated on an overall 50:50
ratio to private contractors after LCB and public agencies
under negotiated contracts. Because of the need to time
the work to coincide with land preparation carried out
1/   Amounts shown are basic costs net of land acquisition costs and
physical and price contingencies and administration and engineering
staff costs (Annex 7).



- 18 -
by the settlers, initial plowing would be carried out
under force account. Land leveling, grading and con-
struction of soil conservation bunds generally would
be carried out by the settlers on a contract basis.
The MDB would carry out work not completed by the
setters.
(d) Roads, Social Infrascructure and Buildings (US$9.2 M).
Most of these works would be constructed under LCB in
accordance with standard Government procedures. How-
ever, about 50% of the buildings would be constructed
by public agencies under negotiated contracts.
5.08      The categories above have been established to utilize, as far as
possible, the Sri Lanka's available construction capacity. It also provides
a means for controlling the cost of negotiated contracts. The total amount
of work under negotiated contracts would be about US$8.5 M or about 24% of
the total civil works cost. Force account work would amount to about
US$0.6 M. The remainder (US$28.7 M) would be awarded to the private sector.
Disbursement
5.09      Disbursements from the IDA Credit would be made for:  (i) 100% of
the foreign expenditures for directly imported equipment, vehicles and spares;
(ii) 50% of local expenditures for imported equipment, vehicles and spares
procured locally; (iii) 100% of the ex-factory price of goods manufactured
locally; (iv) 100% of foreign expenditures for technical assistance and the
monitoring program; (v) 25% of local expenditures for irrigation infrastruc-
ture and roads, and (vi) 47% of local expenditures for social infrastructure
and buildings. Disbursements for force account work under item (v) above
would be made against a certificate of expenditure from GOSL. The documenta-
tion for this would be retained by GOSL and made available for inspection
during project supervision. All other disbursements would be made against
full documentation. The estimated schedule of expenditures, the proposed
allocation of the aid package and an annual disbursement schedule are pre-
sented in Annex 8. It is expected that disbursements would be completed by
December 1982, about six months after completion of the project. Any funds
remaining in the credit account after completion of the project would be can-
celled unless, subject to IDA's concurrence, suitable project related ex-
penditures can be identified.
Accounts and Audits
5.10      An assurance was obtained from GOSL that MDB and other organiza-
tions responsible for implementing any part of the project would prepare and
maintain separate project accounts and financial statements on project ex-
penditures and recoveries thereon, in accordance with sound accounting prac-
tices. Such accounts and financial statements would be audited annually by
independent auditors acceptable to IDA. Certified copies of the audited
accounts and the auditor's reports would be submitted to IDA within six
months of the end of each fiscal year.



- 19 -
Cost Recovery (Annex 12)
5.11      The prime vehicle for recovery of capital costs for the project
would be the Sale of State Lands Law under which the land would be sold to
the settlers. The sales prices would cover the cost of on-farm works and of
special assistance to the settlers. At present, the price is estimated at
Rs 8,880 per family (Rs 3,550 per ac of farm land). Paid over 20 years at 4%
interest, this would require an annual charge of Rs 650 per farm (Rs 260 per
ac) in current terms.
5.12      The O&M costs for the irrigation infrastructure would be recovered
through the Land Betterment Charges Law (LBCL). The'LBCL will, inter alia,
enable the Government to impose water charges in irrigation and drainage
schemes taking into account factors such as amount and dependability of water
supplies, increases in agricultural production, capital and O&M costs. For
the project, GOSL intends to collect a betterment charge of at least Rs 30 per
ac which would be increased gradually over five years to cover full O&M costs
of irrigation works above the field turnout (Rs 80 per ac). An assurance
was obtained that, by January 1, 1978 the Government would complete all
administrative and other measures necessary for imposition and collection
of appropriate charges on all publicly financed water development projects.
5.13      The proposed level of charges, which would give a cost recovery
index of 33% (excluding relevant sunk costs) and rent recovery indexes of 28%
and 33% for upland and lowland farms respectively, is considered appropriate
for the project since: (i) the per capita income of the settlers presently is
below the critical consumption level and would only reach the national average
after full development; (ii) GOSL's present pricing policies for foodgrains
and subsidiary crops are biased against the agricultural sector, and (iii) in
the past, GOSL has not charged for irrigation and there would be considerable
opposition from farmers if the charges were perceived as "too high". An
assurance was obtained that, every three years, GOSL would review, and if
necessary, revise the level of charges to ensure adequate recovery of O&M
costs and as much as possible of the construction costs within the ability
of the farmers to pay in accordance with a cost recovery plan acceptable to
IDA.
VI. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
The Mahaweli Development Board (MDB)
6.01      The MDB was established in 1970 to plan and implement the develop-
ment of Mahaweli Ganga's water resources. It is an agency under the Ministry
of Irrigation, Power and Highways (MIPH), well staffed with experienced tech-
nical personnel, and financed by regular GOSL budget appropriations. The
Board would have overall responsibility for project implementation and settle-
ment as well as agricultural development and the operation and maintenance of
the completed irrigation system. To strengthen its capability in settlement
and agricultural planning and implementation, GOSL intends to appoint an
Additional General Manager (Operations). The present General Manager (GM)
would retain engineering design and construction responsibilities (Annex 9,



- 20 -
Figure 1). The Additional GM (Operations) would be assisted by three Deputy
GMs -- Agriculture, Settlement and Community Development, and Irrigation O&M.
The position of Additional GM (Operations) has been established and his ap-
pointment would be a condition of credit effectiveness.
Project Planning
6.02      The MDB is responsible for planning all aspects  of development in
the project area including irrigation, agriculture, marketing, storage, health
and education. To ensure coordination between 11DB and the specialized depart-
ments that would operate these facilities, GOSL has set up working level tech-
nical subcommittees (TSCs) for sectors other than irrigation. Each TSC is
headed by the Director of the specialized department, with concerned MDB and
department staff as members. The Additional GM (Operations) of MDB would
coordinate the work of the TSCs. At the policy level, a Mahaweli Coordination
Committee (MCC) has been established, chaired by the Secretary, MIPH, with
Directors of the specialized departments, the chairman of the MDB and the
Implementation Advisor (Annex 9, Appendix 1) as members. The committee is
responsible for interagency coordination and for resolving any conflicts
arising at the TSCs.
Project Implementation
6.03      Civil Works and Buildings.  The MDB would be responsible for imple-
menting all civil works and the construction of all buildings through its
Deputy General Manager (Construction). Two field construction offices, each
headed by a Chief Project Engineer, would be responsible for the design of
minor structures, distributary canals, field channels and drains, and con-
struction supervision within their area of operation (typically about 20,000
ac). After the completion of roads and buildings, they would be handed over
to the specialized departments concerned.
6.04      Construction and O&M Equipment.  The MDB would be assisted by the
Department of Machinery and Equipment (DME) in MIPH, in preparing procurement
documents for all construction and O&M equipment. Sri Lanka Trading (Tractor)
Corporation (SLTC) would import all the equipment, including that required by
by private firms that have been awarded contracts, since it is the sole autho-
rized agency for such works. The availability of the required equipment would
be specified by GOSL in the bid documents and the contractors would get an
advance from MDB to buy the equipment from SLTC. The DME would make available
the repair facilities to private contractors. However, contractors would have
the option to select other repair facilities. Public sector agencies that
would carry out part of the works under negotiated contracts would rent the
equipment from DME which is the central pool for machinery and equipment used
for construction of civil works in Sri Lanka.
6.05      Agricultural Extension Services.  The Department of Agriculture
(DA), of the Ministry of Agriculture and Lands (MAL), would be responsible
for the reorganization and strengthening of agricultural extension services
in the project area and for the procurement of equipment, vehicles and supplies



- 21 -
for the extension service. Motorcycles and bicycles to be used by the exten-
sion personnel would be sold by DA on terms and conditions satisfactory to
IDA. The DA would provide a mileage allowance to the extension staff.
6.06      Farm Tractors and Agricultural Equipment.  The SLTC would procure
farm tractors and implements and distribute them to dealers for resale in
the project area. In line with Government policy, the APCs would buy and
operate some farm equipment on a cooperative basis. However, as the APCs
are relatively new and inexperienced, private custom operators selected and
supervised by the APCs, would play the major role. A condition of sale of
tractors to private operators would be that they agree to do custom work with-
in the project area, as specified by the concerned APCs. Credit for purchase
of farm equipment and implements would be provided by the Bank of Ceylon and
the People's Bank. Terms of lending would be 100% financing for APCs and not
less than 75% financing for private operators at prevailing interest rates
(currently 9% to 10% annually) with repayment over five years. Refinancing
for not less than 75% of the total loans for farm equipment would be made
available by the Central Bank of Sri Lanka.
6.07      Other Equipment and Vehicles.  The processing equipment (Annex 6,
Table 6) would be imported by SLTC and sold to CCs and cooperatives on the
same terms as farm tractors. No downpayment would be required. The Coop-
erative Development Department, the Paddy Marketing Board and the Depart-
ments of Health and Education would procure the required equipment for their
use.
Field Organization After Project Implementation (Annex 9, Figure 2)
6.08      A proper coordination between agricultural extension, irrigation
and community development officers is vital to the success of the project.
In order to ensure this coordination, a project management team would be
established. This would consist of a Resident Project Manager (RPM), report-
ing directly to the Additional GM (Operations), and Deputy RPMs for (i) Agri-
culture, (ii) Engineering O&M, and (iii) Settlement, Community Development and
Cooperatives. The Project Agricultural Extension Officer (PAEO) would be
responsible for the extension activities within the area (para 3.11) and report
directly to the Deputy Director of Extension in the Department of Agriculture.
The PAEO, ex officio, would be Deputy RPM (Agriculture).
6.09      Because of the APCs' and CCs' future responsibilities for agricul-
tural planning and water allocation (para 2.11), their boundaries should con-
form with the commands and sub-commands of the irrigation system. An assurance
to this effect was obtained from GOSL. The Community Development Officers
(CDOs), who would be responsible for all settlement aspects of the project,
have been given many of the powers of the Government Agent, including broad
supervisory responsibility over the APCs and CCs. The Agricultural Officers
(AOs) would have to fulfill an important function in giving advice to the APCs.
Since the size of the area under one AO and one CDO roughly corresponds to the



- 22 -
area of an APC, the area of responsibility for one AO, one CDO and one Irriga-
tion Engineer would correspond to the area of one APC. These officers would
be located adjacent to the APC office and they would work closely together.
6.10      At the project level, a Project Advisory Committee (PAC) has been
established. (The legal foundation for the PAC is provided in the draft
Irrigation Law.) The RPM would be chairman of the PAC with the three Deputy
RPMs and senior officers from Anuradhapura District (for example, District
Health Officer) as members. The APCs and the Multipurpose Cooperatives (MPCs)
would nominate farmer representatives as members of the PAC.
O&M of Irrigation Works
6.11      Until local institutions have developed sufficient competence, MDB
would have the responsibility for the O&M of the irrigation infrastructure
down to the field turnout. The CCs, acting under the general supervision
of the APC and advised by Irrigation Engineers of MDB as well as AOs, would
be responsible for the maintenance of field channels and for the distribution
of water among the various users on each field channel. The MDB would oversee
the maintenance operations of the CCs and would have the authority to take
over the O&M of field channels if this was not properly done. The cost for
these works would be assessed against the water users and the required charges
would be collected by the District Government Agent.
6.12      As provided under the Agricultural Productivity Law and the Agri-
cultural Lands Law, the APCs, with the assistance of the CCs, would be res-
ponsible for crop planning within their areas (para 2.11). After completion
of the project, the APCs would work with MDB's as well as the DA's field staff
in preparing the proposed cropping and water delivery schedules. With this
information, the MDB would establish the operating calendars for the various
canals and distributaries. In periods of water shortage, the APCs would re-
vise the cropping schedule in accordance with directives issued by the PAC.
Staffing and Funds
6.13      Assurances were obtained from GOSL that:  (i) all irrigation works,
roads and buildings would be maintained in accordance with sound engineering
practices and that adequate staff and funds would be provided for this purpose;
(ii) adequate funds and personnel would be made available for the agricultural
extension services in the project area; and (iii) all schools and medical
centers in the project area would be adequately staffed within one year after
settlement.
Settlement Procedures (Annex 10)
6.14      The Settlement Planning and Development Division of MDB, headed by
the DGM (Settlement) would be responsible for implementing the settlement pro-
gram, which is based on detailed socio-economic surveys. About 24% of the
area is privately owned and would be acquired by GOSL for settlement pur-
poses under the Land Acquisition Act of 1964, with compensation paid to the



- 23 -
owners. The remaining area is State Land. Farm families already residing
in the project area would be given priority in the selection process, with
the exception of a small number of encroachers on State land who moved into
the area after 1970. The balance of the families to be settled would be re-
cruited from nearby areas. The selection would be based on a detailed points
system, with considerable weight given to agricultural experience. Other
criteria determining the priority are tenurial status, age, marital status
and family size. After final selection, each settler would purchase an
allotted 2.5 ac farm and a 0.5 ac homestead under the Sale of State Lands
(Special Provisions) Law of 1973.
VII. PRODUCTION, MARKETING, PRICES AND FARM INCOMES
General
7.01      The production benefit of the project would principally accrue in
the main project area (about 87%) where a full package of components --
irrigation, on-farm development, production support and social infrastructure
-- would be provided. The cropping patterns and yields throughout the project
area are not uniform at present, since some areas are already irrigated and
some are still underdeveloped (Annex 1, para 2). However, since the climate,
topography and soils are the same, it is projected that, at full development,
the cropping pattern and yields in all areas would be similar (Annex 11).
Agricultural Production
7.02      The project would provide reliable irrigation to both maha and yala
seasons to the main project area, where the crop area would increase from
14,000 ac at present to about 64,000 ac. The lowland soils (40% of the area),
which are suitable only for paddy, would have a cropping intensity of about
185% (95% in maha and 90% in yala). On the upland soils (60% of the area), a
wide range of crops would be grown. These are mostly non-flooded (upland) rice
in the maha and chillies, cereals, pulses and oil crops in the yala season.
The cropping intensity on the upland farms would be about 170% (90% in maha
and 80% in yala). The overall cropping intensity would be 176%.
7.03      With on-farm development, the farmers in the main project area would
be able to effectively control field water application. This, coupled with
improved agricultural extension and other supporting services, would lead to
a rapid build up of yields. Projected yields at full development are estimated
to be at: paddy, 1.4 tons per ac; cereals, 1.0 tons per ac; pulses, 0.5 tons
per ac; chillies, 0.4 tons per ac; and groundnuts/soybeans, 0.75 tons per ac.
The estimated present and projected future (1986) cropping patterns and pro-
duction levels for the main project area are summarized below:



- 24 -
