Vegetables              -         -        1.8           -         -        3.6
TOTAL                  14.0     17.6      64.0          n.a.      n.a.     n.a.
7.04      The areas presently under development would be provided with agri-
cultural supporting services such as farm power and extension. The irrigation
works would be completed by the GOSL without assistance under the proposed
project and the future cropping pattern would be similar to that of the main
project area at full development. The old irrigated area will basically
remain under double irrigated paddy cultivation with a cropping intensity of
150% to 160%. The project would provide extension services to this area which
would not significantly affect the present cropping pattern. It is estimated
that the project would increase foodgrain production in these areas by some
5,600 tons.
Marketing and Prices
7.05      In recent years, Sri Lanka has imported about 50% of its annual
foodgrain requirements of about 1.8 M tons. Despite the Government's empha-
sis on food self-sufficiency, it is likely that Sri Lanka will continue to
be a foodgrain importer for the next decade. At present, the project area
is a rice deficit area. Consequently, the project-related increase of about
42,300 tons of foodgrains at full development would be absorbed readily into
the domestic market. The import ban on pulses and chillies in the early 1970s,
accompanied by a substantial rise in their domestic prices, has reduced signi-
ficantly the per capita consumption of these crops. Thus, the increased
production of pulses and chillies under project conditions would be easily
absorbed by the domestic market. Similarly, no problem is foreseen in mar-
keting the increased production of groundnuts and soybeans in the domestic
market since the demand for vegetable oil is projected to grow rapidly as
a substitute for coconut oil. Prices for economic and financial analyses
are discussed in Annex 13, Appendix 1.
Farm Incomes (Annex 12)
7.06      At present, most farmers in the project area are subsistence ori-
ented. They consume most of their foodgrain and market some chillies and
oilseeds. Non-farm employment such as in construction, transport and
trade is a major source of income during the yala season, when the scarcity



- 25 -
of water prevents the cultivation of subsistence crops. Under project con-
ditions, farm size would be uniform at 2.5 ac but most farmers would be either
on upland (60% of the total) or lowland (40% of the total) soils. Since
different cropping patterns and cultivation methods would be applied, two
farm models have been analyzed. The net farm incomes would be as follows:
Future
Without   Future With Project
Present   Project   Lowland        Upland
Cropped Area (ac)             2.25       2.35       4.40          4.05
Net Farm Income (Rs)         2,100        2,520    5,925         6,855
Income Increase due) (Rs)    -            -        3,405         4,335
to Project    ) (%)                                135           172
Project Rent (Rs)                          -       2,195         2,565
7.07      In the derivation of farm incomes, cash payments for hired labor
and inputs have been charged as production costs. The "future with project"
budgets are based on the assumptions that the farmers would pay on the average
Rs 485 per family in constant 1976 terms for State land at full development
(Annex 12) and the full cost of O&M (Rs 80 per ac) or a total of Rs 565. Pro-
ject rent represents the incremental net income to the farmer when all factors
of production, except water and related services supplied by the project, are
given the rewards that are required for their cooperation.
VIII. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
General
8.01      Land resources in the sparsely populated northern part of Sri Lanka
are highly underutilized and irrigation is essential for intensified cultiva-
tion. The project would help relieve this major resource constraint and
simultaneously assist the GOSL in achieving three basic development objec-
tives: (i) increase food production which in turn would improve the balance
of payment situation; (ii) reduce high unemployment and underemployment
rates; and (iii) improve income distribution.
Production Benefits
8.02      The production increase, due to the project, is estimated at 42,300
tons of foodgrains, 1,100 tons of chillies and 3,800 tons of oil seeds. The
increased foodgrain production would give net foreign exchange savings of
US$8.0 million (in 1977 dollars) per year.
Employment Effects (Annex 13, Appendix 2)
8.03      Increase in labor requirements would be generated primarily by the
expansion of cultivated area and to a more intensive land use in the main
project area. Taking into account the likely growth in farm employment that



- 26 -
would occur without the project, about 12,000 full time jobs would be created
in agriculture. The provision of farm power to other parts of the proj-
ect area would reduce both labor and irrigation requirements during the land
preparation periods. The water savings would be used to expand the cultivated
area, which would lead to increased labor demand for other operations. It is
estimated that these two effects on the labor demand would roughly balance.
Thus, the overall direct employment affects would largely stem from the pro-
vision of irrigation in the main project area. With an increase in the gross
value of agricultural production of some Rs 150 M, the project would generate
additional income and employment in the local non-farm sectors such as market-
ing, transport, and processing. Based on experience from similar areas in
Asia, this would increase non-farm employment in the order of 9,000 man-years.
Income Distribution
8.04      Most of the future settlers -- whether they already live in the
project area or would come from nearby areas -- are poor subsistence farmers
or landless agricultural laborers. Their present incomes, which are fairly
uniform, are about half the national average. Under the project, they would
be settled, with secure tenure, on 2.5 ac farms and the incomes, at full dev-
elopment (in the mid-1980s), would reach the national average.
Social Benefits
8.05      The benefits from social infrastructure are basically non-quantifia-
ble. Likewise, it has not been possible to quantify the benefits accruing to
other areas where population pressure on scarce land resources would be alle-
viated. However, as demonstrated by the failure of many settlement schemes
in Sri Lanka provision of these components under the project would be essen-
tial for the success of the settlement program and help to achieve the pro-
jected production increases.
Economic Evaluation (Annex 13)
8.06      Most of the investments under the Stage I project, which diverted
Mahaweli water from Polgolla to the Stage II area, were prerequisites for
the present project. Stage I created the potential for subsequent invest-
ments, which would have shorter gestation periods and give relatively high
returns. The sunk costs for the Polgolla diversion do not affect the eco-
nomic viability of the proposed project. However, in order to establish
whether the initial decision to provide irrigation to the Stage II areas was
sound or questionable, sunk costs have been included in the analysis.
8.07      The major components of the project -- irrigation, on-farm develop-
ment and agricultural supporting services -- are interdependent; the benefits
from any one depend on whether the others are implemented. Thus, the compo-
nents have to be evaluated as a package. However, since each component of the
package must be justified in its own right, benefits and costs associated
with each component have, to the extent possible, also been analyzed sepa-
rately. The economic rate of return (ERR) for the basic case (i.e. direct



- 27 -
production benefits from the major components of the project) is estimated
at 21%. If sunk costs are included, the ERR would be 14%. For the whole
package, including social infrastructure and sunk costs, the ERR would be
13%. The ERRs for separate component are presented in Annex 13.
Prolect Risk
8.08      In eight years out of ten, the project would provide sufficient
water for the proposed cropping pattern. Irrigation shortages in water
deficient years would range from 5% to 15%, which would have only a minor
effect on the production. Thus, the risk associated with the water supply is
rather small. The ERR of the project is relatively sensitive to construction
delays and to changes in future prices and yields but not very sensitive to
a slower build up of project benefits. The project would remain viable even
under severely adverse assumptions (a combination of (i) 25% decrease in bene-
fits, (ii) 15% increase in construction costs, (iii) two years delay in proj-
ect completion, and (iv) realization of full benefits delayed four years would
give an ERR of 12%). However, there is a need to carefully monitor and con-
trol project costs and to ensure realization of full project benefits.
IX. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
9.01      Agreements on the following points have been reached with GOSL:
(a)  An Implementation Advisor and an On-farm Development
Specialist would be appointed by January 1, 1978
(para 4.12);
(b)  no settlers would be brought into an area earlier than
three months before the expected delivery of water to the.
area (para 4.14);
(c)  a malaria monitoring program would be instituted and, if
necessary, appropriate preventive and remedial measures
would be taken in the project area (para 4.19);
(d)  adequate funds to cover all project cost would be provided
to the agencies concerned for project implementation and
that detailed implementation and financial plans for the
following fiscal year would be submitted to IDA not later
than November 1, each year (para 5.03);
(e)  award of contracts to public agencies would be governed by
the following conditions: (i) unit prices would not exceed
those in the lowest evaluated LCB bid for similar work;
(ii) all contractors would meet prequalification standards
acceptable to IDA; (iii) IDA approves all public sector
contracts before they are awarded, with the exception of
contracts below US$50,000 up to an aggregate of US$500,000,



- 28 -
and (iv) the role of the Department of Machinery and Equip-
ment would be restricted to procurement, renting, repair and
maintenance of equipment (para 5.06);
(f) MDB and other organizations responsible for implementing any
part of the project would prepare and maintain separate pro-
ject accounts and financial statements on project expenditures
and recoveries thereon, in accordance with sound accounting
practices. Such accounts would be audited annually by inde-
pendent auditors acceptable to IDA. Certified copies of
the audited reports would be submitted to IDA within six
months of the end of each fiscal year (para 5.10);
(g) the Government would complete by January 1, 1978 all adminis-
trative and other measures necessary for imposition and collec-
tion of appropriate charges on all publicly financed water
development projects (para 5.12);
(h) every three years, the Government would review and, if
necessary, revise the level of charges to adequately cover
O&M costs as much as possible of the construction costs,
within the ability of farmers to pay in accordance with a
cost recovery plan acceptable to IDA (para 5.13);
(i) the boundaries of the Agricultural Productivity Committees
and Cultivation Committees would be reorganized to conform
with the commands and sub-commands of the irrigation system
(para 6.09);
(j) all irrigation works, roads and buildings would be maintained
in accordance with sound engineering practices and adequate
staff and funds would be provided for this purpose and for
the agricultural extension services in the project area
(para 6.13); and
(k) all schools and medical centers in the project area would be
adequately staffed within one year after settlement (para 6.13).
9.02      Conditions of Credit Effectiveness would be that:
(a) the agreements with co-financiers have been, or are being
simultaneously, declared effective (para 5.03); and
(b) the Additional GM (Operations) has been appointed (para 6.01).



- 29 -
9.03      With the above agreements and together with the bilateral assistance
of US$23.2 M, the proposed project is suitable for an IDA credit of US$19.0 M
under standard IDA terms. The Borrower would be the Republic of Sri Lanka.

ANNEX 1

SRI LANKA
MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT II
The Project Area
General
1.        The project area lies within the Kala Oya basin about 10 mi south-
west of the historic town of Anuradhapura, which is the capital of the north
central province of Anuradhapura and an important road junction. Jaffna,
Trincomalee, Kandy and Colombo are a few of the major towns less than 130 mi
distant by road and rail from Anuradhapura. Some of the important railroads
such as the Colombo-Jaffna and Colombo-Tricomalee lines and two paved primary
roads bisect the main project area. Secondary roads serve remaining portions
of the area.
2.        The proposed project area would cover a contiguous area of 106,000
ac of irrigated and irrigable land and can be divided into five distinct
sub-areas:
Old Irrigated Areas                              Acres      H - blocks /a

Topography
3.        The project area is located in the "dry zone" in north central
Sri Lanka, which is characterized by gently undulating or rolling topography
with rough, eroded catchment areas above the more or less flat lowlands. The
area is generally underlain by a substratum of gneiss or quartzic rock at
fairly shallow depths and rock outcrops are exposed in various locations
throughout the area. River valleys, such as the Kala Oya, have incised stream
channels that drain the area effectively. Slopes range from 1% to 5%, with
an average of about 1.5% to 2% over the greater part of the area. Elevations
range from about 200 to 400 ft above mean sea level.
Soils
4.        The soils are residual (i.e. were developed in place), are derived
from an underlain at relatively shallow depths by granites, and are unusually
low in silt content. As a result, infiltration and permeability rates are
high and water holding capacities are low. Erosion in the uplands has re-
sulted in the lowland soils being somewhat finer in texture and lower in
permeability.
5.        Land classification of the commanded areas, done by the soil survey
of Sri Lanka somewhat along the lines of the United States Bureau of Reclama-
tion method, have indicated that there are: no Class I soils; that most of the
high lands, comprising about 60% of those considered irrigable, are Class II
and Class III; and the bottom lands, are in Class IV, which is considered ex-
cellent for paddy.
6.        The soils of the areas are similar in most respects and are composed
of two main types: (1) well-drained, dark brown to reddish brown soils (Red-
dish Brown Earths or Alfisols) on the crests and upper slopes of the undulat-
ing landscapes; and (2) poorly drained, finer, dark brown to dark grey soils
with pseudo-gley and gley horizons (Low humic Gley, Aqualf suborder of
Alfisols).
7.        The reddish brown earths (RBE) occupy about 60% of the area and the
low humic gley soils (LHG) about 40%. The RBE soils are developed on material
derived from the weathered products of the metamorphic rocks and are underlain
by a gravelly sandy clay loam layer 2 to 4 ft below the surface, which in turn
grades into the underlying weathered rocks.
8.        The most important characteristics of the soils of the project
areas, with regard to development and land use, are: (1) high infiltration
and permeability rates, from a low of 0.17 inches per hour (LHG soils) to more
than three inches per hour (RBE soils); (2) low available moisture holding
capacities (1.2 to 1.6 inShes per foot of soil); (3) high bulk densities
mostly 1.6 to 1.7 gram/cm ); and (4) very low silt contents. The soil clays
31



ANNEX 1
Page 3
are mostly of the non-expanding type (illite and kaolinite), the pH ranges
from 6 to 7, the exchange complex is 80% to 90% base saturated, the silica:
sequioxide ratio is about three, the exchange capacity is 0.5 to 0.6 meq per
gram of clay and the exchange capacities range from 4 to 20 meq/100 grams.
There are some salt and alkali accumulations in low spots where the drainage
is completely blocked. The organic matter content of the surface layer ranges
from 1% to 5% and averages about 2%. Nitrogen and available phosphorus are
low and potash is moderate.
9.        The infiltration rates of the upland soils are above one inch an
hour and of the LHG soils from 0.1 to 1 inch per hour. Thus, water-logged
conditions can exist in either of these soil types only where the drainage
outlets are blockecd. Since the soils retain a relatively small amount of
the water that is available for plant growth (13 to 16% by volume) and the
rooting depth of most crops apparently is restricted, a few irrigations will
be required in the wet season, and more often in the dry season. Because
of their unusually low silt content, the soils are very soft when wet and
very hard when dry and the period when they can be effectively worked for
seed-bed preparation is very short. Hence, it will be necessary to provide
mechanical equipment for timely land preparation.
10.       As implied above, the RBE (uplands) are best suited for the pro-
duction of upland crops such as cereals, pulses, chillies, maize, etc.,
whereas the LHG soils (bottomlands) are suitable only (in the absence of
drainage) for paddy. Recent trials in the project area show that non-flood
(upland) rice can be successfully grown on the RBE soils in the maha season.
Climate
11.       Climatic data for a station at the Maha Illuppallama Research
Station centrally located in the project area near the southwestern tip of
area H4, are shown on Table 1.
Population
12.       The old irrigated areas (35,000 ac) have been fully settled with
11,230 families under the colonization scheme of 1946. The new irrigable
areas (71,000 ac) will be settled with approximately 27,000 families. In
the present situation, sub-area HI is already settled with 2,320 families
of which about 250 families have been brought in from adjacent areas. Re-
sults from a socio-economic survey have indicated that there are approxi-
mately 10,620 families in the Stage II area (excluding H1). Since there
would be approximately 61,750 ac of irrigable land available for allotment
(2.5 ac per family), about 14,000 families would be brought into the area.
13.       The average family size in some of the older irrigated areas is
slightly larger than that in the new irrigable areas (5.6 as opposed to
5.4), due to a larger farm size. Under project conditions, the average
family size in the Stage II area would be reduced, since some of the married
32



ANNEX 1
Page 4
members of the existing families could also apply for purchasing land in
the project area. In addition, the young age distribution of the potential
new settlers (about 35 to 40 years of age) would contribute to a smaller
family. Thus it is assumed that the average family size under project
conditions (in the Stage II area) would be about 5.1.
14.       Present and future population for sub-areas are shown in Table 2.
Existing Settlement
15.       The existing settlement pattern in the project area has evolved
from the irrigation complex of tanks constructed by the Sinhalese settlers
as early as 600 B.C. There are four distinct types of settlements in the
area:
(i) Purana villages: Inhabited by the descendants of the ori-
ginal settlers with a tradition-bound, long history. The
important parts are the village tank, the paddy fields and
the residential unit. These form one composite component
and the highland and chena lands form the other component
and are away from the main village. About 50% to 60% of
the villages or hamlets in the project area are purana
villages. The average number of families living in these
villages is 50 to 60 with a population of about 300 to 400.
(ii) Colonization schemes: In the Stage I area there are four
major colonization schemes, which were started between
1946 and 1964. These are the settlement schemes organized
by the Government on lands which occupy some 35,000 ac
(20,000 ac paddyland and 15,000 ac highland). Under the
project they are covered only by agricultural extension.
(iii) New villages: This recent category generally represents
the overflow from colonization schemes and urban areas.
These new villages are located close to the main roads.
Some 10% to 15% of the villages in the project area are
new villages.
(iv) Urban centers: The largest, Kekirawa, is located on the
fringe of the area (sub-area H7) and its population
amounts to about 4,000. There are also six small urban
centers in or on the fringes of the area. These towns
are places of transit and have better bazaar and public
facilities.

SRI LANKA
MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT II
Water Supply, Demand and Quality
Water Supply
1.        The main sources of irrigation water for the project are the
diversions from the Mahaweli Ganga at the Polgolla diversion site, water
developed by tank storage on various streams (oyas) within the project
area (principally the Kandalama and Kalawewa tanks on the Mirisgoni and
Kala oyas), and return flows from upstream irrigated areas that can be re-
used as supplemental supplies in lower areas. The adequacy of water supplies
in the quantities and at times required by the crops depends on how effec-
tively these supplies are integrated, regulated, distributed and utilized.
2.        The Mahaweli Ganga diversions are conveyed by the improved Sudu
Ganga (Dhun Oya) channel to the Bowatenna Diversion Dam and a portion (a
maximum of 1,000 cusecs out of a total diversion capacity of 2,000 cusecs at
Polgolla) is diverted through the Bowatenna Tunnel to the headwaters of the
Kala Oya, while the remainder flows via the Elahera Canal to the Amban Ganga
service area (Map 11751R). A further division of the Bowatenna Tunnel diver-
sions is made at the outlet portal; out of the total possible diversion of
1,000 cusecs, 700 cusecs would be released to the Dambulu Oya Reservoir and
300 cusecs would be conveyed by canal for a distance of about 3 mi to a
bifurcation structure, where 170 cusecs would be diverted to the Kandalama
tank and the remaining 130 cusecs would flow to the Huruluwewa tank and ser-
vice area. The water released to the Dambulu Oya Reservoir and the Kandalama
tank would augment water supplies developed in the Kala Oya Basin for irriga-
tion use in the H area.
3.        The historic flow of the Mahaweli Ganga at the Polgolla diversion
site during the 1945 to 1964 period varied from an average monthly flow of 115
cusecs in March 1948 to 13,590 cusecs in December 1958. Minimum, maximum and
average monthly recorded flows (in cusecs) during this period are shown
below:
Jan   Feb   Mar   Apr      May    June    Jul   Aug   Sept   Oct    Nov Dec
Min   465   245   115   280       500   1,130    870   315    465  1,810   660   400
Av  1,700 1,020   950 1,370   2,350   4,350  3,520 3,310  2,920  3,840 3,410 2,910
Max 3,130 2,600 2,200 2,300  10,220  12,680  6,850 9,650  6,930  7,460 6,06013,590

ANNEX 2
Page 2
It is evident from the historic record that the full (2,000 cusecs) diversion
capacity of the Polgolla complex could be fully utilized for only about seven
months of the average year, assuming that nearly the entire flow of the river
could be diverted. Downstream water requirements and monthly and seasonal
variations in flow and varying irrigation demands would reduce considerably
the diversions from the Mahaweli Ganga during the remainder of the year.
4.        Actual water diversions from the Mahaweli Ganga at Polgolla were
begun in mid-1976, after experiencing the two lowest years of recorded flow
in the Mahaweli Ganga in 1974 and 1975. Early diversions were limited to
from 300 to 500 cusecs because of extreme low flow in the river. The re-
currence of such a drought period after full implementation of the project
would result in significant irrigation shortages unless holdover storage is
provided in the system, such as contemplated in Stage III development.
Water Demand
5.        Water requirements were based on the design cropping pattern shown
in Annex 11, Figure 1. The crops include flooded rice in LHG soils during
both maha and yala (135 and 105 day varieties, respectively), upland rice in
RBE soils during maha (105 day variety) and pulses, cereals, chillies, ground-
nuts and soybeans in RBE soils, to be grown principally in the yala season.
Cropping intensities of 95% and 90% were assumed for flooded rice in maha
and yala in the LHG (lowland) soils and 90% and 80% for upland crops (includ-
ing upland rice in maha) in maha and yala in the RBE (upland) soils.
6.        Crop water requirements were computed by the Modified Penman method
with grass as the reference crop, using the WATREQ computer program developed
by the Bank. Climatic data for the Maha Illuppallama Research Center, cen-
trally located in the project area, were used in the consumptive use calcula-
tions. Two studies were made: the first to determine an average monthly ir-
rigation requirement at the diversion point based on the average monthly data
available and, the second, to determine irrigation requirements on a monthly
basis using actual data records for use in a simulated project operation study
over the entire period of study. Effective rainfall for the first study was
estimated based on the 75% probability of experiencing average rainfall in
each year (determined separately for both the maha and yala periods). In the
second study, the actual monthly recorded rainfall was used as the base. For
rice grown in paddy, the effective rainfall was limited to 0% of monthly rain-
fall less than 10 mm, 90% for rainfall from 10 to 50 mm, 80% from 50 to 100 mm,
70% from 100 to 200 mm, and 0% for monthly rainfall in excess of 200 mm. For
upland crops (including upland rice), effective rainfall was estimated using
the monthly effective rainfall method (formula) developed by the US Depart-
ment of Agriculture (SCS).
7.        Water requirements for rice grown under flooded conditions (LHG
soils in the lowland areas) would require pre-irrigation for land preparation
requirements. It was assumed that water provided for land preparation would
be sufficient for rice seedling nursery needs in areas where transplanted
37



ANNEX 2
Page 3
rice would be grown. A land preparation period of 20 days was assumed to al-
low time for the land to be worked and for decomposition of weeds and rice
stubble. Soil saturation requirements were estimated to be 50 mm in the maha
and 40 mm in the yala. Evaporation water loss was estimated to be 0.8 of the
average pan evaporation during the 20-day period. Percolation and dike losses
were estimated at 5 mm per day. The flooding requirement for rice to provide
an optimum depth for cultivation was estimated to be 100 mm (four inches).
This water also would provide for consumptive use requirements during the
last two weeks of the cultivation period when irrigation had ceased. Water
requirements for land preparation were estimated to be 50 mm (2 inches) for
upland (irrigated dry) crops.
8.        A field irrigation efficiency of 85% was assumed for the flooded
rice crop during maha and 90% during yala, not including deep percolation
and dike losses (5 mm/day) which are accounted for separately.  Based on field
trials in the project area, a field irrigation efficiency of 30% was assumed
for upland rice:  frequent irrigation would be required and water losses would
be high for rice cultivation in the porous RBE soils.  An average field irri-
gation efficiency of 60% was assumed for the other upland crops. Canal and
distributary conveyance losses (seepage and operational losses) were estimated
to be about 25% (75% efficiency) in the area served by the Kandalama tank.
Canal conveyance efficiencies in the extended Kalawewa service area were
estimated to be about 75% in the lowland areas and 70% in the free draining
RBE (upland) soil areas. Seasonal water requirements for flooded rice are
developed in Table 1. Computer printouts of water requirement calculations
for each crop are shown in Table 2, pages 1 to 12.
9.        The various service (command) areas within the H area are given in
Table 3. The table lists the areas presently irrigated and new areas to be
irrigated with the project, showing both gross and net irrigated categories.
In estimating the net irrigated area, it was assumed that only 90% of the
area to be irrigated would be cultivated and that the remainder would be
taken up in roads, irrigation and drainage works, field bunds and other un-
cultivated areas. A further breakdown between upland and lowland soils is
also shown in Table 3.
10.       Irrigation water requirements and the design of the irrigation
system were made on the basis of rotational irrigation (intermittent flow),
as it would be virtually impossible to supply a uniform, continuous flow to
the small farm holdings in the project area. The extremely small flows in-
volved in continuous irrigation would be too difficult to deliver and manage
effectively and canal losses for the low flows would be high. Therefore,
to conserve water and for ease of operations, rotational irrigation practices
would be instituted and followed in the project area. The basic unit for
irrigation would be the area served by a one cusec turnout from a distribu-
tor canal, averaging about 40 ac in size. A field channel, heading at the
turnout, would convey water to each 2.5 ac farm. Assuming a 3 inch irrigation
38



ANNEX 2
Page 4
requirement and that the full stream (1 cusec) would be available to each
farmer in turn, irrigation deliveries would be rotated between the 2.5 ac
farms on 7.5 hour intervals and water supplied to the field channels would
be rotated every five days. Irrigation scheduling would vary according to
the size of the area served, the crop grown, and the water requirements at
the particular time of service. Irrigation deliveries would be rotated
between the smaller distributor canals during periods of low irrigation re-
quirements for low consumptive use crops.
Water Balance
11.       An analysis of the adequacy of available water supplies to meet
irrigation requirements of the command areas proposed for inclusion in the
Mahaweli Ganga project, based on UNDP/FAO detailed water balance studies for
the 20 year 1944-64 period, was included in the Stage I report (Report PA-29a).
System operation studies were carried out for the six critical (dry) years to
supply ultimate irrigation requirements of the H, Hl, Di and G areas utiliz-
ing Mahaweli diversions at Polgolla, Amban Ganga diversions at Elahera and
the local yield of tanks. Based on the capacities developed for the Polgolla
Tunnel (2,000 cusecs), Bowatenna Tunnel (1,000 cusecs), Elahera canal (1,500
cusecs), H area tanks (195,500 ac-ft), and Di and G tanks (363,000 ac-ft),
irrigation deficits were estimated to occur in five out of twenty years,
ranging from less than 10% (for two years) to 35% (three years).
12.       Detailed studies also were made to determine if a more effective
utilization of Mahaweli flows could be possible by: (1) including reservoir
storage on the Mahaweli Ganga above Polgolla; (2) increasing the size of
the Polgolla tunnel (to 2,500 cusecs); (3) increasing the capacity of the
Bowatenna tunnel (to 1,500 cusecs), and providing storage at Bowatenna. The
studies showed that a significant reduction in irrigation shortages would
occur with deficits in only three years ranging from 20% to 30%, and that an
additional transfer of about 80,000 ac-ft of water to project tanks could
be made by increasing the Polgolla and Bowatenna tunnels to the capacities
shown.
13.       Although the Stage I report strongly recommended that the size of
the Polgolla and Bowatenna tunnels be increased to 2,500 cusecs and 1,500
cusecs, respectively, for the reasons of: (1) increase in water availability;
(2) reduction in irrigation deficits and (3) to provide additional flexibility
in the irrigation distribution system, the tunnels were constructed as initi-
ally planned. A water availability study, made by SOGREAH for the 1945-68
period of study using diversions possible with the existing tunnel capacities
and water developed by the seven largest tanks in the command area, showed
that the irrigation supply developed would be sufficient to supply irrigation
requirements in the total H area (106, 000 ac) plus 10,000 ac in the
Anuradhapura area (computed on the basis of the Bank's cropping pattern and
the water requirement and efficiency criteria assumed by the Bank) in 21
years of the 24 year study period. Irrigation shortages in the three years
of short supply would range from about 20% to 35%.
39



ANNEX 2
Page 5
14.       A monthly water balance study also was made by the MDB engineering
staff using: (1) irrigation requirements based on an assumed MDB cropping
pattern and monthly historic rainfall; (2) estimated monthly diversions from
the Mahaweli Ganga based on the existing tunnel capacities and historic run-
off in the Mahaweli Ganga; and (3) estimated monthly tank yields and return
flows from irrigation generated within the project area. A simulated
operation of the Kandalama and Kalawewa service areas was made to supply
the irrigation requirements of the study areas, using inflow to and regula-
tion provided by the Kandalama and Kalawewa tanks but without considering
storage regulation available in the Dambulu Oya reservoir. The study showed
that irrigation deficits would occur in five years of the 24 year study
period, ranging from about 3% to 15%. With the Dambulu Oya reservoir, the
shortages would be reduced to three years of the study period, in the range
of 5% to 10%.
15.       Based on the simulated operation study, it appears that the water
supply available for project use would be adequate to irrigate the proposed
area. The supply would be reliable and would result in a minimum risk to
the farmers of crop failure due to a short water supply. Although the study
period (1944-1968) did not include the 1974 and 1975 drought years, which are
the lowest flows on record, the inclusion of these years in the operation
study would not change the viability of the project.
16.       The computerized irrigation requirement study made by the Bank,
using historic rainfall and the Bank's proposed cropping pattern and water
requirement and efficiency criteria, supported the study made by MDB.
Considering that the full requirements of the area would be met in 21 years
(88%) of the 24-year study period and that in the remaining three years the
shortages would be less than 30%, the water supply would be adequate for the
area proposed for development. Taking the 1974 and 1975 drought years into
consideration in the study, the area would be fully supplied in 26 out of 31
years (84%), which is within the criteria normally set for irrigation projects.
Quality of Water
17.       The UNDP carried out a study on the quality of the waters of the
Mahaweli Ganga and Amban Ganga and in the Kalawewa and Minneriya tanks. The
tests indicated that the waters of the Mahaweli and Amban Ganga are of
excellent quality, having a conductance of less than 250 micro mhos per centi-
meter, and can be used for irrigation of all soils without developing problems
of salinity. They have a sodium absorption ratio (SAR) of less than ten and
would not pose any danger of alkalinity problems. Tests on water supplies
from the Kalawewa and Minneriya tanks indicated conductance of 250 to 750
micro rnhcs per centimeter. These waters can be used for irrigation of medium
salt: tolerant crops and salinity control measures would not be required pro-
vided drainage is not impeded. The SAR values for these waters are also less
than ten. As the bulk of the irrigation requirements would be met from the
river supplies, the overall quality of water for irrigation can be considered
as good.

SRI LANKA
MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT II
Irrigation Works
General
1.        Stage I of the Mahaweli Ganga project, assisted by the Bank Group
under Loan 653-CE and Credit 174-CE, included works for diverting the flow
of the Mahaweli Ganga River at the Polgolla Diversion Dam through a tunnel
to the Sudu Ganga and further diverting a portion of this water through the
Bowatenna Tunnel to the Dambulu Oya and the Kandalama tank in the H area.
Stage II includes the irrigation and social infrastructure in the 71,000 ac
H area and the necessary equipment, agricultural inputs and extension assis-
tance required by the new settlers to successfully settle and farm the H area.
The current Bank assisted project would provide irrigation and drainage facil-
ities in areas H4 and H5 and complete remaining social infrastructure and
other assistance required in the H area. The engineering plans, surveys,
designs and estimates and construction methods for the irrigation and drain-
age works to be provided by the project are discussed in the following
sections.
Engineering Plans and Surveys
2.        A feasibility study and report for Stage II development was pre-
pared by the consultant firm SOGREAH under the Loan/Credit Agreements for
Stage I. The FAO/IBRD Cooperative Program also prepared a summary report
for developing various tank modernization schemes and Stage II of the
Mahaweli Ganga project. These served as the basis for additional Mahaweli
Development Board (MDB) investigations and plans for implementing Stage II
works.
3.        Initial feasibility planning for Stage II was done using 1:63, 360
scale maps (1 inch: 1 mi) with 20 ft contour intervals prepared in 1946, with
later revisions. Detailed engineering surveys in the H area were started in
1969 by running profile lines on a 1/2 mile grid, using spot levels at five
chain intervals to determine contours and using transit traverse surveys
to locate physical features. Topographic maps at a scale of 1:6,336 (10
inches to 1 mile) with 2 ft contour intervals were prepared for use in
laying out the irrigation system for construction purposes. Enlarged topo-
graphic maps (scale 1:3,168) with a 2 ft contour intervals were made for
farm layout work and for locating field and farm ditches and field drains.
A complementary land use and settlement map at the same scale was prepared
and a closed circuit survey made of each hamlet (scale 1 inch to 2 or 4
chains) to locate each 1/2 ac homestead.
55



ANNEX 3
Page 2
4.        The field surveys for areas H4 and H5 have been completed and
detailed maps for the distribution system layout are scheduled for com-
pletion by March 31, 1977. Additional surveys required to prepare the
detailed topographic mapping for farm layout and irrigation and drainage
system design within field turnout areas (30 to 50 ac unit) would be made
after clearing has been completed in the forested areas.
5.        The preparation of the detailed maps and the general irrigation
system layouts are done by MDB in the headquarters office in Colombo.
Strip surveys with cross-sections every 400 ft are made to prepare maps
used in making construction designs and estimates for the main and branch
canals. The layout of the distributor canals also is done in the head
office but designs are prepared by the field officers under the direction
of the Deputy General Manager (Engineering) and the location of each canal
is checked in the field. A traverse survey is made of each field canal and
drain.
Designs and Estimates
6.        The designs and estimates for works exceeding a cost of Rs 50,000
(major works) are done in the head office of MDB as well as all hydraulic
structure designs. The detailed designs of irrigation works in areas H4
and H5 were begun in January 1976 and are scheduled for completion during
1977.
7.        The Stage II project is designed to irrigate a total of about
71,000 ac of land. The major storage component is the Kalawewa tank, which
commands 58,000 ac of the Stage II area comprising about 18,000 ac on the
left bank and 40,000 ac on the right bank of the Kala Oya. The existing
Righ,t Bank Main Canal (Kalawewa Yoda Ela) is 55 mi in length and provides
water to about 13,000 ac of land, 9,000 ac of which are located in the
upper reaches of the canal. Under the Bank project, a new canal would be
constructed beyond the 17th mile to irrigate 40,000 ac of new land and the
remaining 4,000 ac presently irrigated. The new canal would be 28 mi in
length and would be designed to carrv 1,150 cfs in its upper reach. The
canail would enter four large tanks along its route, which would serve as
equailizing reservoirs. A total of eight branch canals would take off from
the main canal to serve the project area. Both the main and branch canals
would be double banked and lined sections would be provided in rock excava-
tiorL areas. Earth embankments for all canals would be compacted to reduce
leakage from the canals and provide better bank stability. Lining would be
provided for distributor and field canals in reaches constructed in fill
and for main and branch canals in unstable, silty soil areas.
8.        Cross regulators would be provided in the main and branch canals
to provide a minimum head of two-thirds full supply depth at each turnout.
Parshall flumes would be located above and below each tank on the main
canal to determine flow in each reach. Off-takes from the main and branch



ANNEX 3
Page 3
canals would be gated and weirs would be provided to determine flows to
distributor canals. Field turnouts from distributor canals would be gated
and weirs or constant head orifices would be provided to measure flows in
the fie±d canals. The canals and drops, siphons, road crossings, turnouts,
cross regulators and other canal structures are designed in accordance with
accepted engineering criteria to provide required control and measurement of
irrigation flows. The designs are similar to designs prepared for on-going
works in the H area. Irrigation tanks, located along the canals would pro-
vide the additional operational flexibility necessary to achieve high distri-
bution system operation efficiencies. Operation and maintenance roadways
would be provided along one embankment of the main, branch and distributor
canals. The main, branch and distributor canals would flow continuously
during the irrigation season and flows in the field canals would be rotated
by turnout units (usually from 30 to 50 ac each). The MDB designs and opera-
tional plan assume furrow irrigation in upland soils on a 12-hour daylight
schedule, with unattended irrigation deliveries during the night to lowland
paddy areas. This would require careful and continuous canal operation for
changing irrigation requirements and a proper balancing of upland and low-
land soils in each turnout unit. Field irrigation efficiencies would be low
if irrigation water applications were irregular or unbalanced. Also, rota-
tional deliveries by field canals would require the same crops or that crops
with similar water requirements be grown by farmers within each field turnout
area.
Construction Methods
9.        Main and branch canals would be constructed by private contractors
or by public construction agencies, using equipment-intensive construction
methods. The first 18 mi of the new Right Bank Main Canal is presently under
construction or will be under contract by the end of 1976. The remaining 10
mi would be included under the Bank project. The construction of distributor
canals, drains field canals and on-farm distribution canals and drains would
be let in small contracts to local contractors, including the farmers them-
selves where qualified, and would be principally labor-intensive. In clearing
forested areas, brush and trees up to 1 ft girth would be cleared by hand,
trees 1 ft in girth to about 6 ft girth would be cleared with chain saws, and
larger trees would be cleared with heavy equipment. The trees and brush would
be piled and allowed to dry for about one month before burning. Two burning
operations would probably be required, one for clearing by hand labor and with
chain saws and the other for trees and stumps cleared by machine.
10.       Ripping and stump removal would be done by machine, as well as the
smoothing of small mounds or elevated areas during the ripping process. Rip-
ping and cross ripping would normally be done 12 to 15 inches in depth. The
MDB wouild provide one initial plowing of all farm areas after arrival of the
settlers to assist the settlers to complete land grading and bunding on their
own farms. Land preparation work not completed by the settlers, due to the
difficulty of the work or the unwillingness of the farmers to complete the work,



ANNEX 3
Page 4
would be completed by MDB. Land leveling or grading work on lands with
slopeBs up to 2-1/2% generally would be completed by the settlers themselves
under contract to MDB.
Irrigation and Drainage Works Provided Under the Project
11.       The irrigation and drainage works to be provided by the project are
list,ed in Table 1. These works and related construction activities required
are summarized below:
(a) jungle clearing;
(b) ripping, cross-ripping and smoothing of mounds
or elevated areas in cleared areas;
(c) initial plowing of farms to facilitate land prep-
aration by the settlers;
(d) construction of remaining 10 mi of main canal, with
necessary structures;
(e) construction of branch canals and structures;
(f) construction of distributor canals and structures;
(g) construction of field canals and structures;
(h) on-farm-development works, including land forming
and grading, bunding, and construction of farm
ditches and drainage channels;
(i) improvements to village tanks; and
(j) provision of main, secondary and turnout drains and
structures.
March 1977

SRI LANKA
MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT II
Production Support
General
1.        The proposed project would complete Stage II of the Mahaweli Ganga
Development Program. Stage II would provide irrigation to 71,000 ac of
largely undeveloped land and settle about 27,000 families. To fully utilize
the potential created by the irrigation infrastructure, agricultural produc-
tion would be supported by: strengthened agricultural extension services and
applied research; farm equipment; marketing and processing facilities, and
market roads.
Agricultural Extension
2.        Present Situation.  The Department of Agriculture (DA) operates a
multiplicity of extension programs that are all supervised by the Deputy
Director of Agriculture (DDA), Extension. Its general, country-wide program
works through the District Agricultural Extension Officers (DAEOs) in each of
the 22 Districts. The District extension organization primarily assists
farmers in areas that have not received any special benefits from irrigation
projects or any other form of land improvement. Irrigation projects, special
land development projects and schemes to promote the production of specific
crops are usually supported by their own extension service with only informal
links to the regular District extension service.
3.        The project area principally falls within the Anuradhapura District
which has the following extension staff at present: 1/
1 District Agricultural Extension Officer (DAEO)
1 Deputy DAEO
7 Subject Matter Specialists (SMSs)
3 Agricultural Officers (AOs)
42 Agricultural Instructors (AIs)
147 Village Level Workers (Sinhalese acronym is KVS, standing for
Krushikarma Viyaptha Sevaka).
4.        Extension activities in north-central Sri Lanka are technially
supported by the Maha Illuppallama Research Station. However, contacts are
not formalized and depend entirely upon the personal relationships of the
officers. Maha Illuppallama is also the site of a training center, which
runs in service and orientation training courses for all levels of staff, but
thEse activities are impeded by a lack of adequate facilities.
1/   The functions of these officers are described in para 9.
60



ANNEX 4
Page  2
5.        Besides little exposure to up-to-date research, the extension
services suffer from: a lack of clearly defined responsibility; the
absence of fixed schedule for farmers' training; serious shortage of
transportation for all levels of staff and a lack of accommodations -- all
of which hamper the recruitment of key staff for field operations.
6.        Reorganization of the Extension Services under the Project.  The
project area is located in the Districts of Anuradhapura, Kerunegala and Matale.
Besides the three District extension organizations, a couple of special exten-
sion projects are active within the project boundaries. To ensure a uniform
extension service in the area and to facilitate the coordination with the
irrigation and community development staff, it was recently decided to create
a special extension District for the project area (106,000 ac). Staff for
this special District is presently being recruited.
7.        The proposed staffing is:
New Areas      Old Areas
(71,000 ac)   (35,000 ac)
Project Agricultural
Extension Officer (PAEO)                     1
Additional PAEO                              I
SMSs                                         8
AOs                                          8              3
Al Field                                    26             14
AI (attached to APC Office)                  8
KVS Field                                  104             30
KVS Assistant to AI and APC                  8
8.        The staffing density for new areas is based on:  one KVS per 250
farmers; one AI per 1,000 farmers, and one AO per 3,5000 farmers. This density
is high but it is justified because the proposed farming systems would include
new techniques and require very careful attention to water use and management.
Thinning of staff would be done as the program progresses. Since the farmers
in the old areas have experience in irrigated cultivation, the staffing den-
sity in these areas would be lower than in the new areas.
9.        The proposed extension organization is illustrated in Chart 16495.
The functions of the new staff would be:
Project Agricultural Extension Officer (PAEO). The PAEO would be
in overall charge and responsible for the work performance and training of
all the staff. He would, ex officio, occupy the position as Deputy Resident
Project Manager (DRPM), Agriculture. He would be assisted at project head-
quarters by an additional PAEO who would have charge of the day-to-day opera-
tional activities of all extention workers. The APAEO would serve principally
as a staff officer. Both these officers would be agricultural graduates with
several years operational and technical experience.
61



ANNEX 4
Page 3
Subject Matter Specialists (SMSs). To enable maximizing output
and to utilize fully expensive and scarce inputs there would be SMSs for:
--   farm machinery maintenance and operations;
water management and irrigation practices;
extension methods;
farm management;
paddy;
maize and sorghum;
other upland crops, and
--   plant protection.
The SMS would be a university graduate with at least two years specializa-
tion in his subject and at least three years general field experience. His
functions would be:
(a) field work, in support of staff in contact
with farmers;                                                60%
(b)  training courses (lectures and demonstrations);              20%
(c) contact relevant research work and workers,
and                                                          10%
(d) preparing material for publication and training
courses administration, work planning -- short-
and long-term                                                10%
Agricultural Officers (AO). Each AO would be responsible for one
Agricultural Productivity Committee (APC) area, reporting directly to the
PAEO and supervising about three AIs. He should preferably be a university
graduate with at least five years' experience, capable of planning, organ-
izing and supervising the extension activities of his staff. He would be
well acquainted with problems facing the farmer in the field of crop produc-
tion and farm management.  The planning of the KVS, AI and farmer training
programs would be his specific task. In this he must have access to, and the
assistance of, the SMSs. He would spend three days a week in the field,
visiting his AIs and ensuring that he sees, in rotation, all his KVSs and all
their work areas. The task and the problems in hand would determine whether,
and which, SMS accompanies him during the visits.
62



ANNEX 4
Page 4
Agricultural Instructor (AI--Field). One AI would supervise
about four KVSs. He would have at least two years training at an agri-
cultural school and three years' field experience. He would be sufficiently
well versed in the production of all field crops, plant protection and
water management to be competent in back-stopping his KVSs. He would, in
turn, visit each of his KVS's farm groups. He would participate in the
fortnightly KVS training sessions and take personal charge of demonstration
plots and the field days conducted on them. He would help organize and take
part in the periodic farmers' extension meetings.
Agricultural Instructor (AI--APC). One AI would be posted to each
APC; his office would be located in the APC complex. He would advise and
assist the chairman and the members of the committee in their function of
ensuring proper and efficient farming practices, which would include the
preparation of the annual production program. He would be assisted by one
KVS.
Village Level Workers (KVSs). The staff having the most frequent
contact with the farmers would be the KVS. They must have a rural, farming
background and preferably also formal training in a special training insti-
tute. They would have suitable transport to travel within this area and
would be subjected to frequent practically-oriented training courses devised
and run by first and second-line supervisors. They would have, within their
areas, initially about 250 farmers. Methods of operation would be centered
around a prearranged and rigidly followed schedule of visits, known to all
farmers in the area. They would return to the same outlet command every week.
The KVS would visit, and work with the same contact farmers for at least one
season. The contact farmers would all have been carefully selected as re-
spected leading farmers of the area. Two groups of six contact farmers would
be a suitable number for the one day's visit. Other farmers having land in
the same outlet commands would be encouraged to participate. Success would be
measured by the number who follow the KVSs' recommendations and by the results
they achieve. The regular visiting schedule would enable the KVS to assist
farmers in matters pertaining to input distribution and administrative proce-
dures. During every farm visit, only the specified extension tasks of the
period would be discussed. Other problems would be dealt with on a special
day set aside for the purpose or passed on to their supervisor.
10.       Staff Training.  Training would be continuous for all levels of
extension staff. The KVSs would receive regular fortnightly training. Between
sessions, they would communicate to the farmers no more than the two or three
key points of crop husbandry that they, themselves, were taught during the
fortnightly courses. The KVS training may well take place in the field to
ensure that all participants are actually capable of doing the task that
they would be called upon to teach and demonstrate.
11.       Before each season, there would be a one week intensive training
course for the KVSs, held at the Maha Illuppallama Training Center. The
syllabus would consist of lectures on the major crops, discussions on
63



ANNEX 4
Page 5
problems encountered, any new production techniques and the most effective
extension methods to use. The participants would have the opportunity of
utilizing past experience and newly acquired knowledge and skills to draw
up work plans for their areas for the following six months. In developing
these plans, emphasis would be placed on the priorities determined, practi-
cability and focusing of efforts. A number of these programs would be pre-
sented by their planners for analysis as well as in general discussion.
This, in itself, would be an important exercise.
12.       There would be a one-day training session each month for all the
AIs during which the previous and the following months' activities would be
discussed. There would also be presentations on special problems and some
practical training. A seasonal six day seminar of AIs would be organized
before each maha and yala season.
13.       The program for the graduate staff would cover not only technical
matters, but also practice of supervision, organization, management and
extension methods. It would include discussions with agricultural research
groups, management groups from other projects, and staff from the Institute
of Management and the Institute of Public Administration.
14.       Training of Contact Farmers.  The expected impact, of, and on, the
contact farmers cannot be achieved by KVS visits alone. Further training
would develop their abilities and make the work of the KVS more effective.
The training would take the form of practical demonstrations of the crops
they are growing, to improve crop husbandry practices by emphasizing key
factors such as timely weeding, fertilization, pest control, water management,
and most importantly, irrigation methods. Farmers would be taught the most
suitable cropping pattern for their conditions, as it was developed or has
evolved in their own area. This training would be in the form of short
courses taking place either at the Training Center in Maha Illuppallama
or at one of the APC centers. The courses would last two to three days and
be held before each season after the KVS courses.
15.       Monthly meetings of these same farmer groups would be organized by
the KVS and AI with the participation of the Cultivation Committee. The
meetings would serve as a forum for discussions concerning crops and farm
problems in general. Guidance by the AI and the KVS would highlight the
specific tasks to be undertaken during the following weeks.
16.       Project Support.  In order to support the reorganization of the
extension services, the project would provide: (Annex 4, Table 1 and Annex 6,
Table 4):
(a) bicycles for KVSs;
(b) motorcycles for AIs;
(c) field vehicles for AOs, SMSs and PAEOs;
64



ANNEX 4
Page 6
(d) demonstration equipment for the extension service;
(e) offices and quarters for extension staff, and
(f) equipment, dormitories and other buildings for the
Maha Illuppallama Training Center
Agricultural Research
17.       Present Situation.  Agricultural Research is headed by the Deputy
Director of Agriculture (DDA), -- Research -- who is based at headquarters of
the Department of Agriculture, Peradeniya, near Kandy. There are 16 research
stations in the country, two of which are significant to the project --
Batalagoda where almost all the paddy breeding is done, and Maha Illuppallama
where both irrigated and rainfed farming systems and agronomic improvements
are being investigated.
18.       Sri Lanka has always had a good rice breeding program.  From the
period 1960 to 1970, the "H" series of locally bred varieties replaced most
of the traditional varieties. Since the advent of modern varieties (HYV),
Sri Lanka has successfully incorporated the IRRI strains to produce varieties
suitable to her own conditions. Of the varieties released, BG 11-11, 90-2,
34-6, and 34-8 are outstanding. The most notable feature of some of these
new varieties such as 34-6 and 34-8 is the short growing season (three to
three and a half months) which maximizes utilization of rainfall in the yala
season. All these varieties have a desirable plant type and are resistant to
blast.
19.       Research on other crops and cropping systems for the dry zone is
concentrated at Maha Illuppallama, located in the project area. Considerable
progress has been made in introducing and developing new varieties of chillies,
green gram, black gram, pigeon peas (dhal) and cow-peas. Water management and
drainage studies have been carried out under different conditions, for these
crops and for rice. These studies have covered items such as: irrigation
frequencies; furrow lengths and consumptive use of water.  The research find-
ings from tIaha Illuppallama have been applied to two pilot projects within the
project area.  These projects test cropping patterns under irrigated conditions
and farmers' acceptance of new agronomic practices.
20.       Proposed Research Project.  A new Irrigation and Water Management
Pilot Project would be set up in the project area. It would be jointly spon-
sored by the Irrigation Department, Agriculture Department and MDB. It would
involve:
(a) farming techniques and cropping patterns suitable
for the soils, topography and climte of the dry
zone;



ANNEX 4
Page 7
(b) water management techniques, with special attention
given to irrigation requirements, frequencies of
application, drainage methods and changes in soil
properties under prolonged irrigation; and
(c)  farmers' acceptance of new crops and improved irri-
gation and cultivation practices.
The scheme would also be used as a demonstration area for extension workers
and contact farmers.
21.       Project Support.  To support research activities of the pilot
scheme, the project would provide (Annex 4, Table 1 and Annex 6, Table 4):
(a) four-wheel and two-wheel tractors;
(b) laboratory and field equipment
(c) field vehicles for research staff, and
(d) offices and quarters for research staff.
ARricultural Equipment
22.       Farm Power--Present Situation.  In the project area, about-two-
thirds of the land preparation for the tank irrigated paddy fields is done
by bullocks. The remainder is done by four-wheel tractors and, to a lesser
degree, by two-wheel tractors. Land preparation for chena cultivation is done
exclusively by manual labor. However, in the more developed parts of the dry
zone,, about four-fifths of the land preparation is done mechanically, since
the soils are extremely hard when dry and timely land preparation is required
for high yields and optimum use of scarce water resources.
23.       The four-wheel tractors, most of them in the 35 to 40 hp class,
use mainly a tined cultivator (tiller) and only occassionally, under dry con-
ditions, a disc plow. The two-wheel tractors use a reversible mould-board
plow and a rotary cultivator. The buffaloes use a barpoint plow with a long
sole which helps to seal the surface on which it travels, thereby impeding
percolation and assisting with water conservation.
24.       Farm Power Requirements under the Project.  The farm power and
requi.rements in the area would increase about nine-fold under the project.
The number of draft animals is small and the stock increase will be slow. The
Department of Agriculture has plans for a buffalo breeding and training pro-
gram which could lead to an increase in the working buffalo population in the
project area. However, in view of the island-wide shortage of buffaloes, it
is assumed that the bulk of the primary and secondary tillage would necessar-
ily have to be done by tractors.
66



ANNEX 4
Page 8
25.       It is estimated that about 30% of the future needs for land
preparation could be met by the project area's existing farm power and
from sources outside the area. The remainder of the requirements would
have to be provided under the project. The future requirements have been
estimated using the following output factors:
Working

26.       Project Support.  The project would provide 300 four-wheel and
1,200 two-wheel tractors with implements and 50 motorized and 1,300 hand
knapsack sprayers (Annex 6, Table 5).
Storage, Marketing and Processing
27.       Seeds -- Present Situation.  Farmers obtain their seeds via the
extension service and the Multi-Purpose Cooperative Societies (MPCSs), which
get them from Government farms and from certified seed growers. There is
a great deal of farmer-to-farmer exchange and many cultivators save their
own seed, especially for paddy. An unofficial certification scheme for
paddy seed is operating, which is similar to that of the International Seed
Testing Association in relation to standards, and recognizes foundation,
registered and certified seed. Foundation and registered seed are always
grown on Government farms; certified seed is mostly produced by certified
growers. The system works well for paddy, as illustrated by the rapid
spread of new varieties. Pulse seeds usually present serious storage and
quality control problems due to the rapidly decreasing germination rate.
Thus, it would be desirable to introduce statutory controls and standards
for pulse seeds. However, the GOSL is presently advised on seed quality
control problems by a team from FAO.
28.       Fertilizers -- Present Situation.  All fertilizers are imported
by the Ceylon Fertilizer Corporation (CFC) which also prepares most basic
NPK mixtures. The imports are expected to be reduced in the future because:
(a) The GOSL plans to construct a urea plant, to be in
operation by 1980, which would have an annual capa-
city of 300,000 tons, and
67



ANNEX 4
Page 9
(b) Sri Lanka has deposits of some 25 million tons of
rock phosphate (30% P205) and GOSL intends to use
these deposits for the production of super phosphate
and diammonium phosphate.
29.       Fertilizers for field crops are sold at controlled, subsidized 1/
prices primarily through the MPCs, with the distribution to the cooperatives
handled by the Department of Rural Institution (RI). A minor part of the
fertilizers is sold either directly by the RI or by the APCs and CCs. Pri-
vate dealers tend to specialize in fertilizers for a small number of cash
crops.
30.       Plant Protection Chemicals -- Present Situation.  Active ingre-
dients and emulsifiers for all pesticides are imported; most of the formu-
lation takes place in Sri Lanka. Locally established representatives of
two major companies have access to proprietary products and handle some 75% of
the market; most of the non-proprietaries are handled by the Ceylon Petroleum
Corporation which is also the only importer for solvents. The range of
pesticides available is comprehensive and adequate, and packaging is good.
The GOSL controls both the retail price and the range of products that can be
sold. (permissions are given first after successful field tests). However,
there is no statuatory control over storage conditions or shelf life, but such
legislation is now under consideration. The private sector firms have their
own Field Advisory Services and run demonstrations on farmers' fields. They
have a network of sub-distributors at the village level and also supply MPCs
and APCs.
31.       All formulations are liquid, therefore, they must be applied
through sprayers, which are usually rented from a neighbor or from the MPC.
The weed killers are systemic, and are effective if put on the leaves of
the weeds growing in water or on any part of the weed if there is not
starLding water. Insecticides, especially for plant hoppers, must be applied
in small droplets and very uniformly. Many of the sprayers are not of a
design that would ensure this.
32.       Storage and Marketing -- Present Situation.  Marketing of paddy
has been handled by the Paddy Marketing Board (PMB) and to some extent by
the private traders. As a result of the recent removal of legal restric-
tions on the movement of paddy, the activities of private traders has in-
creased. However, due to their limited storage facilities, it is expected
that: the PMB would continue to play an important role in paddy marketing.
1/   The present rate of subsidy is about 50%.
68



ANNEX 4
Page 10
33.       The PMB buys paddy from farmers at a fixed price (presently
Rs 33/bu) through a vast network of purchasing points run by the coopera-
tive societies. The Village Level (Branch) Cooperatives get a commission
of Rs .75 per bushel from PMB for grading, weighing, and sacking. Grading
is normally done according to the following standards.
Grade I        Grade II
Chaff                                 9%            12%
Foreign matter                        1%             2%
Other varieties                      10%            10%
Moisture content                     1.6%           16%
The difference in price is Rs 1.15 a bushel in favor of Grade I
(very little Grade II is purchased). Since the purchase centers are not
equipped to test for quality variations, grading is mostly based on judg-
ment and experience.
34.       The periods of purchase are between March and July inclusive,
and again between August and October. The peak period is between March
and May, when the transport is sometimes a bottleneck which results in an
accumulation of produce at the purchasing centers and temporary suspension
of purchases. From the point of purchase, the paddy is taken to the PMB
stores. At present all is flat storage; the first silos are now under
construction. There is no drying facility, no forced ventilation, although
the stores are well designed for natural ventilation. Average storage time
is about four months and annual turnover is only one time a year.
35.       Marketing of maize and sorghum is handled mostly through private
channels. The GOSL, through the Oils and Fats Corporation and PMB, also
purchases maize and sorghum for feed purposes or for blending with imported
wheat flour. Recently, as the farmers are encouraged to grow maize and
sorghum, the PMB is compelled to purchase these crops without limit at the
guaranteed prices.
36.       Marketing of other crops such as pulses, and chillies, are mostly
through private channels. Per capita consumption has fallen to about one
third of the amount used before the import ban in the early 1970s as a
result of a significant increase in prices. Consequently, no problem is
foreseen in marketing the increased production of these crops in the future.
37.       Processing -- Present Situation.  Paddy milling is the only major
processing industry in the project area; any sorghum or maize processing
that may be necessary would take place either in flour mills or Oils and
Fats Corporation feed mills, outside the project area. Rice milling is a
two-tier operation. The small village husking mills work on a custom basis
to meet domestic requirements. 1/ Large commercial privately-owned mills
1/   Provision is made in the village layout for the private sector
under project conditions.                                               69



ANNEX 4
Page 11
handle the other 20% but their share will increase in time because the
private sector is not allowed to replace its mills. The village mills
seen were of poor quality and in a poor state of repair. Practically all
the grain is broken and much is wasted with the husks. The larger mills,
although all are better maintained and waste little grain, do not polish
too finely and also have high breakage. Because of the superficial polish-
ing, milling out-turns are about 70%. About 10% of the milled rice is
parboiled.
38.       Project Support.  Existing facilities in the project area would
be inadequate for handling the projected nine-fold increase in agricultural
production. The project would provide (Annex 4, Table 2 and Annex 6,
Table 6):
(a) 50 seed stores;
(b) 50 fertilizer stores;
(c) 50 paddy collection centers. Each collection center,
located at each village center, would require about
100 tons storage (based on storage of 25% of estimated
crop collection) and working space for a simple auger-fed
bagging shute about 1,000 sq ft of floor space. The
building would have large doors at each end and a load-
ing dock at the end opposite the bagging unit.  A moisture
tester capable of giving instantaneous readings would be
available.  Samples above 16% moisture would be accepted
at a discounted price and then only if they could be
delivered to a drier the same day;
(d)  buildings for three new primary MPCs and 24 new branch
cooperatives, funds for the renovation of 26 existing
branch buildings. Vehicles (for transport of inputs and
paddy) for the new primary cooperatives and vehicles and
minor equipment for the Cooperative Development Department;
(e) buildings, plant and equipment for a PMB processing complex
(with a milling capacity of 1,000 tons per month and a
storage capacity of 5,000 tons); and
(f) rice hullers, threshers/winnowers for the 50 Cultivation
Committees in the project area. These units would be
available for hire to farmers.
Aricultural Credit
39.       Present Situation.  The main institutional sources of agricultural
credit for the farmers in the project area have been two state-owned banks --
the People's Bank and the Bank of Ceylon. Private lenders also have an



ANNEX 4
Page 12
important role in supplying credits due to their flexible terms and condi-
tions as well as simplicity of operation (about 85% of the total credits).
However, their roles have been largely confined to consumption rather than
production loans.
40.       Two types of loans are provided to the farmers:  (i) short-term
loans for crop cultivation at 6% to 9% rate of interest and with a repayment
period of 180-270 days, and (ii) medium-term loans for purchases of farm
power and agricultural implements at 9%-10% rate of interest and repayment
period 2 to 3 years.
41.       A farmer who is a member of a cooperative may apply for a loan
from the People's Bank through the Cooperative Rural Bank (CRB) of the MPC.
He may also apply for loans directly from the branches of the Bank of Ceylon
by presenting an Agro-Identity Card. This card which contains his identity,
his acreage as well as his outstanding indebtedness in other banks would
have to be cleared by the CRB. If he is found to be a defaulter, his appli-
cation would be rejected.
42.       Upon the approval of the loan from either one of the institutions,
the farmer would receive money for expenditure items requiring cash payments
such as land preparation, harvesting and threshing. Others, like fertilizers
and pesticides would be issued in kind by the MPCs and the APCs. Terms and
conditions of a loan from both banks are comparable. However, it takes a
shorter time to process a loan with the Bank of Ceylon than with the People's
Bank since the former has direct contact with the farmers.
43.       The default rates have been running on the average at more than 50%
of the outstanding loans during the last three years. One of the main rea-
sons for this is the practice of the writing-off of defaults on outstanding
loans with reimbursement up to 75% from the Central Bank of Sri Lanka to the
other banks. The rate of recovery showed a very wide range between the CRB
concerned and between crop years, reflecting variations in farmers' ability
to pay from year to year as well as the capabilities of the respective bank
managers in collecting loan repayments. The default rate of the Bank of
Ceylon is lower due to its stricter control in the determination of a loan.
However, it may also be due to its recent entrance into providing rural
credit whereas the People's Bank has been operating the loans since 1967.
44.       Recent legislation would improve the default situation.  The Bank
of Ceylon would provide loans only to farmers and for purposes approved by
the APCs who are also empowered to collect the loan repayments. The Agri-
cultural Insurance Law would compensate for crop failures which would pro-
vide the farmers with cash to repay the loan in unfavorable years.
45.       Proposed Credit Arrangement under Project Conditions.  The project
would provide four-wheel and two-wheel tractors, as well as sprayers,
threshers, and winnowers to the project area. Credit facilities for the
purchase of farm equipment would be provided by the Bank of Ceylon and the
People's Bank. Terms of lending would be 100% financing for APC and not
71



ANNEX 4
Page 13
less then 75% financing- ior private operators at prevailing interest rates
(currently 9% to 10% annually) with repayment over five years (currently
three years). Refinancing for not less than 75% of the total loans for farm
equipment would be made by the Central Bank of Sri Lanka to the Bank of Ceylon
and the People's Bank. The terms and conditions are satisfactory to IDA.
Market Roads
46.       The project area is well provided with good all weather roads.
However, the secondary road network in the main project area (40,300 ac)
would require improvements to cater to the increased needs after the area
has been fully settled. The project would provide 25 mi of new link roads
and improvements to about 50 mi of existing link roads (Map 12362 R). The
link roads would be built according to the Department of Highways' "Class D"
on a 20 m wide right of way, with a formation width of 7.5 m, a carriage
way width of 3.6 m and a pavement depth of about 28 cm. The link roads
would be maintained by the Department of Highways.
