Social Infrastructure
Present Situation
1.        Settlement in the project area, at present, largely consists of
colonaization schemes and purana type villages (Annex 1). Social infra-
structure like schools, medical facilities and post offices are scattered
throughout the area. The colonists who are settled on the more developed
land (Stage I, old irrigated area) are better served with these facilities
than traditional villagers. However, on the whole, social services in the
area are considered more advanced that that in similar areas of other South
Asian countries.
2.        There are three levels of schools in the project area:  primary,
junior, and senior secondary schools. Primary schools (Grades 1-5) are
usually within an average one mi walking distance. However, the walking
distance is about five to eight mi for junior (Grades 6-9) and senior
secondary schools (Grades 10-11). Class size is about 30 to 35 students
for 'primary schools and 20 to 25 students for higher level. Education is
free for all students while fees are collected for operation and maintenance
of non-classroom facilities such as playgrounds, sport centers, and libraries
(about Rs 6 per year per student). Primary school teachers are normally re-
cruited from the villages whereas the higher level teachers come from cities.
Quarters are usually provided to the principal and the vice-principal and
in some cases to teachers.
3.        The health services are provided through a network of medical
institutions of varying levels, the largest being provincial hospitals
with specialist services located at the District level serving a population
of 70,000 to 100,000 and smaller institutions such as central dispensaries
at tihe village level, serving 8,000 to 10,000. There is a system of referral
in whnich cases from small institutions are sent to larger institutions by
an ambulance service. There is also a public health field program which
sprays anti-malaria chemicals about three times a year. Preventative health
care for mothers and children is undertaken by public health nurses, mid-
wives and inspectors under the overall charge of a Medical Officer of Health.
Family planning services are integrated with maternal and child health ser-
vices. Partly because treatment feeds are nominal, with no fee charged for
in patient and Rs .25 charged for out-patient treatment, medical serviOes
are frequently used by villagers.
76



ANNEX 5
Page 2
4.        Other services such as bus and postal faclilities and village wells
are scattered throughout the project area. These facilities are considered
adequate to serve the present population.
Proposed Settlements
5.        Under project conditions, the landholding pattern would be re-
organized into 2.5 ac farms and 0.5 ac homestead lots in the new land (71,000
ac) whereas there would be no change on the old irrigated area (35,000 ac).
Since the existing families would not fill up all the available irrigable
lots in the new land, additional families would be brought in. The number
of area families would increase from 25,270 to 39,350 after project im-
plementation.
6.        Settlements in the new land would be arranged in accordance with
the irrigation layout. The basic unit would be an irrigation sub-command
(or block) with about 1,250 ac of irrigable land corresponding to the
Cultivation Committee (CC) boundaries and would consist of about 500 farmers.
Within this sub-command area, farmers would be grouped into hamlets of
about 100 farmers each. This contrasts with the settlement pattern in the
old colonization schemes with a grouping of about 500 families. This large
grouping was found to lead to alienation and a lack of social cohesion.
7.        Each group of about five hamlets would have a village center
where most of the supporting services such as branch cooperatives, storage
facilities and CC offices would be located. The village center -- serving
about 500 families -- would be situated within the most central hamlet of
the group. The average distance from any homestead to the village center
would be about three miles. Settlement layouts would incorporate the
existing situation throughout the project area to the new design in order
to displace as few homesteads as possible.
8.        The largest service center would be the township which would
correspond to an irrigation command area and an APC area -- serving about
3,000 to 4,000 families. It would have service facilities such as primary
cooperative buildings, APC offices and milling plants. A detailed descrip-
tion of social infrastructure at all settlement levels is given in Table 1.
Project Support
9.        Existing facilities are adequate to serve the present population.
However, additional facilities have to be provided for some 14,000 new
families to be settled in the area. The project would provide the following
components.
(a) Schools. About 600 classrooms in primary and junior secondary
schools (Grades 1-9) and 20 classrooms in senior secondary
schools (Grades 10-11) together with laboratories, playgrounds



ANNEX 5
Page 3
and quarters. The proposed number of classrooms are
based on the standard national planning criteria of the
GOSL Education Department which would be responsible for
operating and maintaining these facilities. Details are
provided in Tables 2 and 3.
(b) Medical Centers. Two rural hospitals and two central
dispensaries with maternity wards as well as vehicles,
equipment and staff quarters. The required medical
facilities are based on the national planning criteria
of the GOSL Medical Department which would be responsible
for operating and maintaining these facilities. Details
are provided in Tables 4 and 5.
(c) Community Centers. A community hall to each village
center. The center would be used for village meetings
and training sessions (i.e. water management, farm
practices, and home economics) as well as for cultural
and recreational activities. These facilities would be
operated and maintained by MDB. These activities would
be administered by community development staff who are
also responsible for the settlement aspect of the project.
The staff would be provided with vehicles and housing.
A description is in Table 6.
(d) Village Roads. About 200 mi of roads are required to be
upgraded or built within the hamlets throughout the main
project area (H  and H5, 40,300 ac).  These roads would
be simple, 15 ft wide, and graded but not metalled. Drain-
age and culverting would be supplied where required. Details
are provided in Annex 7, Tablc 1. Village roads would be
maintained by MDB.
(e) Hamlet Wells. About 1,235 new wells would be required to
serve additional families in the project area. One well
would serve about 20 families. The wells would be main-
tained by MIDB.
March 1977

SRI LANKA
MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT II.
Organization and Management
The Mahaweli Development Board (MDB)
1.        The MDB, an autonomous agency under the Ministry of Irrigation,
Power and Highways (MIPH), was established in 1970 to oversee the develop-
ment of Mahaweli Ganga's water resources. It has the responsibility for
preparing final plans, designs, specifications and tenders, for awarding
construction contracts and for supervising the implementation of the pro-
ject. The Board is financed by regular budgetary appropriations by the
Government.
2.        Because of the nature of its previous work, MDB has been oriented
to engineering design and construction and the Board is now well staffed
with experienced technical personnel. However, since the present and future
stages of the Mahaweli Development S heme would require a greater emphasis
in the settlement and agricultural  3pects of project planning and imple-
mentation, reorganization of the M-f: would be proposed (Figure 1).  A de-
scription of the reorganization is >,7ovided in the main text (para 6.01).
Field Organization after Project Implamentation
3.        Since the project would con,Litute the first major development
of unirrigated land in the Mahaweli Master Plan, a proper coordination be-
tween agricultural extension, irrigation and community development officers
would be vital to the success of the project. The proposed organization at
the project level is detailed in Figure 2 and a description is provided in
the main text (para 6.08-6.10).
Construction Implementation
4.        All works under the project are scheduled for completion by June
1982 as shown on the Implementation Schedule (Figure 3). Details on comple-
tion of surveys and topographic mapping for the irrigation and drainage system
and designs and estimates of the irrigation works are given in Annex 3.
Irrigation infrastructure would be completed by July 1980, with irrigation
works in the H4 area being implemented and completed shortly before similar
works in H5. Construction of the drainage system would be delayed until
drainage requirements are more fully identified; however, it is expected that
this work would be completed by late 1981. The first 28 mi of the Kalawewa
Right Bank Main Canal is presently under construction and the remaining 10 mi
under the Bank project would be completed by December 1979. Land preparation
101



ANNEX 9
Page 2
work would be initiated after arrival of the settlers and the work would
continue through early 1982. Land clearing and ripping of forested areas
would be among the first work initiated (using equipment presently in the
country) and would be completed in both areas by June 1979. The improvement
and construction of market roads in the project area would be completed by
June 1981. The construction of social infrastructure would be started by
July 1978 and those works necessary for the immediate comfort and well being
of the settlers would be in place when the settlers arrive. All social in-
frastructure would be completed by June 1981. Administrative buildings,
workshops and quarters would be completed as rapidly as possible for use by
supervisory staff during construction of the project. Other buildings would
be constructed according to individual program requirements.
5.         The Deputy General Manager (Construction) under the direction of
the General Manager (Design and Construction) would have direct responsibility
for construction of the project. Two field construction offices would be set
up; one in the H4 area at Tambuttegama, and the other in the H5 area at Gala-
wewa. Four separate construction units would be established in the H4 area
and three in the H5 area. Staffing requirements for these offices would be
as shown in Figure 4.
Operation and Maintenance (Figure 5)
6.         Operation and maintenance (O&M) of the total H area (except for the
previously developed H3, H6, H8, and HIO-12 areas which are operated by the
Territorial Civil Engineering Organization) would be controlled by eight
range command offices under the direction of the Resident Project Manager.
Each range office would be responsible for irrigation and drainage facili-
ties serving about 9,000 ac, as well as reaches of the main canal and/or
irrigation tanks that fall within the range boundary. Regional sub-command
offices, two in each range, would be directly responsible for irrigation
releases and canal maintenance for about 1,500 ac. Ditch riders (Jala Palana
Sevaka - JPS), operating from the regional offices, would distribute water
from the distributor canals to the field channels, controlling up to 500 ac
each.
7.         Irrigation requirements for each season would be determined by the
Project Advisory Committee (PAC) (Figure 2). The CCs would make recommenda-
tions with respect to cropping calendars and water requirements for irrigated
lands under each turnout. Water requirements for each service area would be
determited and operating calendars would be established for the various
canals. The estimated requirements for the project area would be submitted
through the DGM (Water Use Allocation) to the Water Management Panel, who
would make water allocations for power and major irrigation command within
the Mahaweli system. Irrigation deliveries would be rotated between field
channels (turnouts) and between farmers served by each field channel. The
MDB plan of operation assumes that upland areas would be irrigated 12 hours
per dlay during daylight hours and that the water either would be released to
102



ANNEX 9
Page 3
lowland paddy areas during the night or stored in tanks in areas where tanks
are available. This assumes a proper balance between uplands and lowlands
in each turnout area or a flexibility of operations that is not necessarily
available. In order to achieve project irrigation efficiency levels assumed
for project formulation studies, irrigation deliveries would have to be made
on a 24 hour rotational basis as soon as the farmers are trained in this
method of irrigation.
8.        Proper operation, repair and maintenance of the irrigation and
drainage system would require modern equipment, which would be provided under
the project (Annex 6, Table 3). Vehicles and telephone communication in the
project area would be required for effective control and delivery of project
water supplies, and heavy equipment would be required to maintain the system
and make emergency repairs.
9.        Operation and maintenance costs of the irrigation and drainage
system in the H area are estimated to be about Rs 80 per ac or US$11.00 per
ac (Table 1), which would provide an excellent standard of operation and
maintenance of project facilities.
Technical Assistance
10.       Development of the Mahaweli Ganga Project is proceeding rapidly
and MDB's capabilities to plan, design, supervise, construct, operate and
administer the irrigation and drainage facilities and related programs of
the expanding project are under considerable strain. Equipment limitations
and the small number of qualified construction concerns in Sri Lanka would
require the maximum utilization of in-country resources and a high level of
management planning ability to complete on-going works and the new project
facilities as scheduled. Management assistance to MDB would help to in-
sure that scheduled completion dates are met. Similarly, project benefits
would be fully realized only upon the final implementation of on-farm ir-
rigation and drainage facilities and the timely completion of the leveling,
grading and land preparation work proposed for the particular crops to be
grown. Assumed irrigation efficiencies are dependent upon well executed
and technically sound development of upland areas for irrigation, expertise
for which is lacking in Sri Lanka. The MDB's predominant orientation to plan-
ning and construction would gradually shift to operation and management of
the completed works, where additional expertise would be required to develop
proper irrigation delivery schedules and establish preventative maintenance
measures.
11.       In order to strengthen MDB's capabilities in these fields, the
project would finance for a three-year period the services of one implemen-
tation advisor and one on-farm development specialist. Draft terms of re-
ference for these specialists are provided in Appendix 1.
103



ANNEX 9
Page 4
12.       The services of a Hydrologist for short-term consultant help (several
trips totalling about 12 months) to assist with the hydrologic monitoring pro-
gram would be financed by the project (see Appendix 2).
Monitoring and Project Performance
13.       The project would provide funds for a program for monitoring project
performance. The program is outlined in Appendix 2.
March 1977

SRI LANKA
MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT  II
Terms of Reference
Implementation Advisor
1.        An implementation advisor would assist the chairman to integrate
all activities of MDB and coordinate all aspects of MDB's program. The ad-
visor should have extensive experience in planning and implementing major
multipurpose water development schemes, including work in such fields as
engineering, agricultural development, settlement planning and operations.
He would:
(a) provide direct assistance and advice to the chairman,
MDB, in integrating the complex and diverse activities
performed by MDB;
(b) provide guidance in the scheduling and performance of
construction work for project facilities and in the
implementation of related work programs;
(c) advise on all phases of project implementation and
develop work flow programs and methods to fully utilize
available resources to complete works and programs on
schedule;
(d) provide assistance in developing a sound operation and
maintenance program for project irrigation and drain-
age facilities and in setting up a water management
training program;
(e) assist in developing future construction programs and
manpower requirements for these programs; and
(f) provide guidance in the studies of the overall Mahaweli
development scheme.
On-Farm Development Specialist
2.        The specialist would work with the engineering staff of MDB and
would provide assistance in implementing on-farm development works and in
developing practical irrigation schedules. He should be experienced in
111



ANNEX 9
Appendix 1
Page 2
tropical, monsoon-type irrigation and have a solid background in design, con-
struction and operation of irrigation systems. He should be able to develop
criteria for designs and provide guidance in the layout and construction of
all necessary on-farm development work. In providing assistance to MDB, he
would:
(a) review existing plans and procedures for on-farm devel-
opment and recommend changes to improve both the planning
criteria and implementation methods, where this would
result in better water management and land use;
(b) assist MDB in designing and constructing on-farm distri-
bution and drainage facilities and in establishing leveling,
grading and land preparation criteria for developing the
2.5 ac individual farms;
(c) assist and train project personnel and farmer groups to
carry out rotational irrigation and make recommendations
to improve irrigation schedules; and
(d) provide advice and help to monitoring program personnel
in studies of field irrigation efficiencies.
112



ANNEX 9
Appendix 2
Page 1
SRI LANKA
MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT II.
Monitoring and Project Evaluation
Objectives
1.        The Stage II of the Polgolla Complex is the first development of
new lands under the Mahaweli Master Plan. It is designed to create a de-
velopment pattern that can be multiplied in the future. Thus, the objec-
tives of the monitoring component would be to:
(a) evaluate the development model, and
(b) provide data for refining the technical design para-
meters for future stages.
Scope
2.        The monitoring studies functionally can be divided into four major
groups:
(1) Water Use.
(a) continuous flow measuring program;
(b) estimation of irrigation efficiencies.
(2) Agricultural development.
(a) annual compilations of cropping patterns and yields
using routine data;
(b) in depth analysis of yields in areas where field
irrigation efficiencies are estimated; and
(c) in depth bench mark surveys.
(3) Indirect employment effects.
(4) Social adaptation and institutional development.
113



ANNEX 9
Appendix 2
Page 2
Staffing
3.        The water use studies would be carried out by MDB field staff, with
intermittent assistance from a consultant in hydrology (twelve man-months).
The annual compilation of cropping patterns and yields would be done by the
agricultural extension staff, partly utilizing data supplied by APCs and CCs.
The remaining studies would be contracted to the Agrarian Research and Train-
ing Institute (ARTI).
Steering Committee
4.        A small Steering Committee would be set up to coordinate the moni-
toring studies. It would consist of:
Additional GM (Operations), MDB, Chairman
DGM (Agriculture), MDB
DGM (Settlement), MDB
DGM (O&M), MDB
RPM
Director, ARTI
Director, Maha Illupallama Research Station
Director, Irrigation and Water Management Pilot Project
DetaiLed Components of the Studies
5.        Continuous Flow Measuring Program.  About 50 flow recording stations
would be installed at selected sites to monitor: water releases through the
Bowatenna Tunnel; the amount of water released from the major storage tanks;
and water distribution to selected branch canals, distributaries and field
channels. For branch canals and distributaries, where no recording stations
are installed, approximate stream measures would be used to estimate the water
releases to each irrigation block. Reports on such releases would be sub-
mitted annually to IDA.
6.        Estimation of Irrigation Efficiencies.  The data from the flow re-
cording stations would be used for estimating: conveyance and operational
efficiencies for various types of canals; field conveyance and field applica-
tion efficiencies below the turnouts for both upland and lowland areas.
7.        Annual Compilation of Cropping Patterns, Yields and Impact of
Exten,sion Services. This data would be collected by the agricultural exten-
sion staff in cooperation with the APCs and the CCs. Besides yields and
cropping patterns, information would be collected on the number of contact
farmers and "followers" and estimated yield differences between "followers"
and farmers who do not follow extension advice. Such reports would be sub-
mitted annually to IDA.
114



ANNEX 9
Appendix 2
Page 3
8.        In-Depth Analysis of Irrigated Yields.  In those turnouts where
field conveyance and field application efficiencies are monitored, produc-
tion of various crops would be fully enumerated in order to establish the
yield - irrigation relationship under normal farm conditions.
9.        Bench Mark Surveys.  Three in-depth bench mark surveys would be
carried out (1977, 1981 and 1985) by ARTI. They would be on a stratified
sample basis and cover, for upland and lowland areas: cropping patterns;
yields; input requirements; use of hired labor; farm incomes; non-farm
incomes and their sources; marketing channels and prices; family members'
age and occupation; farm capital owned; credit channels, and interest rates.
10.       Indirect Employment Effects.  Sample surveys would be made to: es-
timate the number of non-farm households and their sources and levels of
income; obtain enumeration of agricultural processing facilities in the pro-
ject area, and to establish the numbers and types of commercial establish-
ments in the area. The amount of non-farm employment would be related to
both the population of the area and its total agricultural population. The
surveys would be carried out in 1977, 1981 and 1985.
11.       Social Adaptation and Institutional Development.  In 1981 and
1985, ARTI would carry out interviews with members of APCs, CCs and MPCs,
Government officials and a sample of settlers to evaluate the administra-
tive set-up for the project. The surveys would cover items such as: ade-
quacy of social services; community participation; conflicts between old
population and new settlers, and the role of project management in the de-
cision process.
12.       Evaluation Reports.  Based on the annual monitoring reports and
the special surveys, ARTI would prepare two comprehensive evaluation reports
to be submitted to IDA by June 30, 1982 and June 30, 1986.
115



ANNEX 10
Page 1
SRI LANKA
MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT II
Settlement Procedures
General
1.        Under the project, the settlers would be provided with a 2.5 ac
farm and a 0.5 ac homestead lot. Since some of the areas are privately
owned and some are State owned but encroached upon, all the land in the
project area would have to be acquired and reorganized in order to ensure
equitable distribution. The responsibility for the settlement process rests
with the Settlement Planning and Development Division of MDB, headed by a
Deputy General Manager (who is also appointed as a Deputy Land Commissioner).
The settlement procedure is described below.
Socio-Economic Surveys
2.        Subsequent to soil and land classification, irrigation surveys
and dtelineation of the area to be developed, social-economic surveys are
conducted. These have been completed by MDB in cooperation with the Agra-
rian Rlesearch and Training Institute (ARTI) over 24,600 ac of the area and
in oultline over sub-areas H4 and H5 (49,300 ac). These surveys are designed
to coLlect basic data and information on such matters as population, land
ownership and tenure, agricultural practices, income levels and infrastruc-
ture facilities. The data also provides the number of eligible farmers
likely to become settlers and the number of settlers to be recruited from
outside the project area.
Land Acquisition
3.        About 24% of the total land area in the project area is privately
owned or leased from the GOSL; the remaining 76% is State land. In order
to plan an orderly layout of the irrigation system and associated settle-
ments and to assure equal land ownership, GOSL has decided to acquire all
lands within the project area (including all homestead lands) in accordance
with the Land Acquisition Act as amended in 1964.
4.        The basis for land acquisition is a Government declaration pub-
lished in the Gazette (a Government Publication) that the land is needed
for a public purpose. After an inquiry into the market value of the land,
the acquiring officer of the District determines the amount of compensation
to be paid. The act allows appeals against the amount of compensation but
not against the decision of the Government to acquire the land. The Govern-
ment can take possession of the land even if such appeals are pending. For
116



ANNEX 10
Page 2
the Mahaweli Stage II area, the compensation has been set at Rs 200 per ac
for upland areas and Rs 300 per ac for paddy land. Homesteads are subject
to individual valuation, taking the size and quality of the buildings into
account.
5.        Whenever any land becomes vested in the Government, under the pro-
cedure described above, it becomes "State land". Any person who might occupy
such land without authority becomes an encroacher on State land and subject
to eviction.
Settler Selection
6.        Farm families already residing in the project area are given prior-
ity in selection with the exception of the illegal encroachers who moved
into the area after June 1, 1970. The latter are estimated to be about 9%
of the existing population. To ensure a socially homogeneous population and
to maintain existing social links, the balance of settlers would be recruited
from nearby areas. The selection would be based on a detailed points system,
with highest weight given to agricultural experience. Other criteria deter-
mining the priority would be tenure status age, marital status and family
size.
Sale of State Land
7.        The basic law regulating the sale of State Land is the Sale of
State Lands (Special Provisions) Act of 1973, as augmented by the Regula-
tions of the Minister of Agriculture and Lands in 1975. Under the law, the
District Government Agent (or his representative) makes the selection of
the settlers. Appeals against the selection are decided by the Land Com-
missioner, whose decision is final.
8.        The settler can either pay the price for the lot in full and
receive a "grant" for the land or pay the price in equal installments over
20 years with 4% interest and receive a "permit" for the land. When
the payment is completed, a grant would be substituted for the permit.
Under both systems, the lots can be subdivided through sales, with prior
approval by the Government Agent, or through inheritance. However, for the
project, no fragmentation below one acre would be permitted.
9.        Permit land cannot be leased to any other person and grant land
can be leased only under certain circumstances such as prolonged illness.
Joint ownership is not permitted and the land can only be mortgaged to
certain specified institutions, such as the People's Bank or the Bank of
Ceylon.
Allocation of Land
10.       Approved settlers who have been farming land in the area would be
given first choice of land and they would normally be allocated farms in the



ANNEX 10
Page 3
same area as they farmed before. Similarly, new settlers would be given
choices of land and be grouped together with their friends. In the event
that no mutual agreement would be reached, balloting would be done.
11.       Each settler would be given 2.5 ac of irrigable land in one unit.
Most farms would be situated on one of the principal soil types; either on
the upland soils (RBE) or on the lowland soils (LHG).  The former are more
permeable and suitable for mixed cropping. The latter are suitable pri-
marily for paddy cultivation. However, the income earning potential of the
two Earm types would be similar. Using the design cropping pattern of 170%
cropping intensity on the upland farms and 185% cropping intensity in the
lowland farms, the respective net family income at full development would
be albout Rs 7,400 for upland 1/ and about Rs 7,600 for lowland farms.
The upland farms moreover could quickly adapt to market opportunities due to
the varied cropping possibilities compared with the lowland farms, which would
specialize in paddy. In addition to the 2.5 ac farms, each settler would be
given 0.5 ac of homestead (upland and non-irrigated) where he would reside and
have the opportunity to grow tree crops, vegetables and rear livestock.
12.       The price to be paid by the current settlers in respect of alien-
ations in 1975 and 1976 has been fixed by GOSL at a total of about Rs 8,800.
Future sale prices may be varied by GOSL in the light of any increased cost
of development, however, expressed in constant terms, they are expected to
remain at the present level. The basis for the current sale price has been
calculated as follows:
2.5 ac farm land plus 0.5 ac homestead lot            Rs
Undeveloped value of the land                         600
Jungle clearing and stumping                        2,500
Leveling and farm layout                            1,300
Field channels                                      3,800
Special assistance /a                                 680
Total               8,880
/a   See para 14.
Establishment of Settlers
13.       The entry of settlers on the land would be timed so that irrigation
water would be available to each individual farm within three months of entry.
1/   Average of two projected financial farm gate prices.



ANNEX 10
Page 4
At entry, all land would have been cleared, stumped and cross-ripped (where
necessary in forested areas) and also would have been given a single plowing
to help with land shaping. Where land shaping is necessary, the settlers
would be given the choice of doing the work themselves (manually) or having
it done by MDB. If they do it themselves, they would be paid at unit rates
by MDB. If the works would not be completed within two years, the work
would be taken over and completed by MDB. Settlers would also be paid for
construction of soil conservation bunds and other on-farm works required
such as drainage ditches. (The cost of the above on-farm development would
be included in the purchase price of the land, see para 12.)
14.       The settlers would also be given the following special assistance
(to be repaid):
Materials for temporary hut          Rs 100
Seed and planting materials             250
Field tools                             100
Subsistence allowance @ 50/month
for five months (cash payment)        250
Total             680
World Food Program (WFP)
15.       Under an agreement between GOSL and WFP, every settler family (up
to a maximum of five members) would receive free food aid for a period of
12 months. The present agreement expires in March 1977 but it is expected
that it will be extended. The monthly amount per a family of five persons
is approximately as follows:
flour         92 lbs.
dhall           8 lbs.
dried fish      8 lbs.
skim milk       5 lbs.
sugar           3 lbs.

SRI LANKA
MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT 'II
Cultivation Practices, Cropping Patterns and Yields
Genera:L
1.        The proposed project covers an area of 106,000 ac of which 35,000
ac are old irrigated land and 71,000 ac are undeveloped land. Irrigation
infrastructure has been completed by the GOSL for 6,100 ac (Hi) of undevel-
oped land and it is presently under construction for 24,600 ac. The remain-
ing 40,300 ac of undeveloped land would be provided with irrigation infra-
structure under the proposed project. At full development, the cultivation
practices, cropping pattern and yields of the land under development would
be similar to that in the old irrigated area. Since most of the production
benefits of the proposed project would come from the irrigation development
of 40,300 ac of undeveloped land, the discussion below is confined to this
area.
Present Situation
2.        Irrigation Cultivation.  Paddy is the main irrigated crop grown
during both maha and yala seasons. Maha paddy is broadcast, row-drilled
or transplanted during October-January and harvested during January-April.
Yala paddy is planted between February and April and harvested during May-
July. Transplanted paddy is not popular with the farmers in the project
area due to the lack of assured water supply. Cultivation is mostly carried
out by manual labor except for plowing, tilling and leveling which is
generally done by buffaloes, four-wheel tractors and to a lesser extent,
two-wheel tractors. Harvesting and threshing are also not fully mechanized.
3.        Since the soil is very hard when dry, a considerable amount of water
is used in field preparation (saturation and leveling). Because of this
practice, land preparation in the maha season usually does not begin until
the tanks are sufficiently filled to ensure a crop. This results in a delay
of the maha planting season and consequently, insufficient use is made of rain-
fall. During the yala season, the soil is softened and less water is required
for land preparation. However, yala planting is also delayed partly by the
delays in starting the maha paddy crop and partly by the need to harvest
crops planted on the slash and burned (chena) land (para 4).
4.        Chena Cultivation.  Slash and burn (chena) agriculture is practiced
in nearly all jungle areas. Clearing and land preparation commences in June
or July, depending on the nature and density of the vegetation, and is norm-
ally completed in August, followed by burning. Sowing and planting on the
120



ANNEX 11
Page 2
chena land commences with the rains towards the end of September and extends
to the end of November. Cereals (kurakkan, maize and sorghum) are normally
planted first, followed by chillies and pulses (principally cow peas). Because
of an unreliable water supply from the tanks to ensure a paddy crop, most farmers
look upon chena cultivation as their major source of food. For this reason,
only when the chena crops are secured does the farmer and his family transfer
attention to the paddy lands. In the meantime, much of the rainfall has been
wasted. In addition, since some of the farmers are likely to plant paddy as
early as when the maha season commences, irrigation of late planted paddy is
difficult to correlate with the requirements of paddy planted earlier. This
results in increased irrigation losses and wastage.
5.        Chena Crops.  Cereals crops such as kurakkan, maize and sorghum are
grown in the maha season (under rainfed conditions). Sorghum has recently been
expanding as good varieties have become available from India and these ratoon-
well in Sri Lanka. Maize has not been grown successfully in the area due to
pest and bird damage as well as its succeptibility to waterlogging in periods
of heavy rains. However, investigations at the Maha Illupalama Research Center
show that both sorghum and maize can be grown successfully under irrigation,
especially, on the more permeable soils. Pulse crops such as cow peas, black
and green grams and pigeon peas are presently grown under rainfed conditions
but trials have been made to grow them following the paddy crop on the irri-
gated lands. This is supported by the good results from the Maha Illupalama
Research Center. The chillies crop which is also grown under rainfed conditions
has become an important cash crop in the area due to the ban on its imports
to Sri Lanka. However, the widespread planting of chillies is constrained
by its high labor requirements as well as intensive use of fertilizer and
pesticides. Oilseed crops such as sesamum are the only crops grown during
yala season due to their low water requirement. This is mainly used to
provide cash income to the farmer.
6.        Present Yields.  Information on present irrigated paddy yields
was obtained from socio-economic surveys conducted by the MDB in January
1975 and from surveys conducted by the Agrarian Research and Training Insti-
tute (ARTI) in Anuradhapura District. The average yield for the project
area during the maha season is estimated to be 0.8 tons per ac. The water
supply in the yala is more unreliable and yields are somewhat lower--about 0.7
tons per ac. Present yields for other crops are based on field observations,
discussions with farmers and extension and research workers and by reference
to survey data.
Future Without Project
7.        Under the "future without project" conditions, water supply in
the tanks would increase slightly due to the return flow from irrigation
of nearby areas. This would lead to a small increase in the yala paddy
acreages and yield in addition to the historic average yield growth rate
(about 2% per annum). It is expected that the areas of chena crops would
increase slightly as some would become stabilized rainfed crops. Yields of
chena crops would increase about 2% per annum.                            121



ANNEX 11
Page 3
Projected Cropping Pattern and Yields under Project
8.        The project would provide irrigation in both maha and yala seasons
to a net irrigated area of 36,300 ac (gross 40,300 ac). Thus, it is projected
that the cultivated area would increase from 14,000 ac at present to about
64,000 ac with the project. The lowland soils (4% of the area), which are
suited only for paddy, would have a cropping intensity of about 185% (95% in
maha aLnd 90% in yala). Due to the high permeability of the upland soils (60%
of the area), a wide range of crops would be grown, mostly non-flooded (up-
land) rice in the maha and cereals, pulses, chillies and oilseeds in the
yala season. The cropping intensity of the upland farms would be about 170%
(90% in maha and 80% in yala). The overall cropping intensity would be 176%.
9.        The main factors which would contribute to increased yields under
the project would be: the increase in quality and reliability of the irriga-
tion supply; the provision of drainage; better cultivation practices result-
ing from a reorganized extension service, as well as an improved distribution
of agricultural inputs (machinery, seeds and pesticides). Research station
yields at Maha Illupalama between 1970 and 1974 have indicated that, if
sufficient water is available, the paddy yields in both the maha and yala
seasons are about equal. However, in the case of maize, sorghum and pulses,
the yields during the yala season would exceed those during the maha. The
difference is due, in part, to more hours of effective sunshine.
10.       Considering the above information as well as farmer practices and
the findings of surveys by the ARTI, it has been concluded that, approximately
five years after the completion of project works (assuming a strengthened ex-
tension service), average yields would be on the following order:
Long Tons per Acre
Maha          Yala
Rice Lowland (broadcast)    1.41            1.41
Rice Upland (drilled)         1.27            -
Cereals                      0.90           1.10
Pulses                       0.50           0.55
Oilseeds                      _             0.75
11.       The proper management of irrigation deliveries would permit pre-
season preparation of maha crops only in September. Maha sowing would be
from September through October for harvesting in January and February. Yala
sowing would be in March through April for harvesting in June through July.
Other changes in agronomic practices expectd with the project would be:
122



ANNEX 11
Page 4
(a) Land preparation for a given unit would be done with mechanical
power within a short time, preferably while dry or following
the first monsoon rains. This would decrease the demands on
stored water for pre-irrigation to soften the soil.
(b) Firm irrigation seasons would be established. This would en-
able shortened crop cycles over the entire service area but re-
quire much greater discipline of farm operation than at present.
(c) Farmers would be trained in proper water management techniques
for efficient use of the irrigation supply. Efforts would be
made to educate the farmers in rotational irrigation (Annex 2)
practices on a 24-hour delivery schedule to further conserve
water and to reduce waste.

projected areas, yields and production are described in Table 1.

SRI LANKA
MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT  II
Crop Inputs and Farm Budgets-
General
1.        Present agricultural practices in the project area and the expected
overall changes in cropping patterns, yields and production due to the project
are described in Annex 11. This Annex discusses the impact on the individual
farm. The purposes of this micro-analysis are:
(a) to estimate the farm incomes for representative farm sizes
in order to determine whether the prospective increases
in farm income would be sufficient to compensate the
farmer and his family for the additional effort and for
other production costs they would have to incur;
(b) to determine the distributional impact of the project
and to compare the income level of the project area
farmers with those of farmers in other areas, and
*(c) to analyze the effects of different Government policies
regarding water charges.
Crop Input Requirements
2.        Present and project unit input requirements under the project
are given in Table 1. The estimated current levels of inputs are based on
interviews with project area farmers and on information obtained from the
MDB f-ield staff, the Department of Agriculture and from several surveys in
similar areas of Sri Lanka. Some improvements in farming practices are as-
sumed for future "without project" conditions. Future requirements under
the project are based on the recommendations by the Department of Agricul-
ture and on observed cultural practices of the progressive farmers of the
area. For the different inputs, the estimates are:
(a) Land Preparation -- Much of the land preparation work in
the area is done on a custom basis. At present land prep-
aration in the upland areas (mostly chena cultivation)
is done by manual labor while in the lowland areas, trac-
tors or bullocks are used. These practices are assumed
to remain basically unchanged in the future if the
1/   This Annex was prepared before the March 1977 re-evaluation.  *The
re-evaluation will have no gignififant effect on the conclusion
reached in this Annex.
126



ANNEX 12
Page 2
project is not implemented. The future "with project"
estimates assume that land preparation is done either with
tractors or bullocks for both upland and lowland areas.
For a detailed discussion on the cost estimates of land
preparation, see Annex 13, Appendix 1.
(b) Fertilizers -- The estimated present and future usages of
fertilizers in relation to the recommended applications
for irrigated crops are shown below. It is not expected
that usage will reach an overall 100% of the standard crop
recommendations with the exception of Nitrogen in the case
of paddy.

(c) Farm Labor -- Labor requirements for each operation and
crop are presented in Table 1. These estimates are based
on the assumption that land preparation is mechanized with
the introduction of the project. Consequently, labor re-
quirements for land preparation "with project" conditions
will be reduced compared to present and future "without
project" conditions. However, the increase in yields would
increase labor requirements for harvesting and threshing.
The hired labor components are estimated at 30% of total
labor requirements for paddy and chillies and 20% for
other crops. The hired labor is valued at the average
market wage rate of Rs 5.5 per man-day under the "present"
and "without project" situations and Rs 8.2 per man-day
under "with project" conditions. 1/
1/   Increase in demand for farm labor under "with project" conditions will
increase the market wage rate (see para 17).
127



ANNEX 12
Page 3
Crop Budgets (Table 2)
3.        Crop budgets are based on the financial prices of outputs and inputs
discussed in Annex 13, Appendix 1. The present subsidized prices for fertil-
izer are used for present crop budgets while no subsidies are assumed for the
future.
Choice of Farm Size
4.        Under the present condition, most of the farmers in the project
area cultivate both paddy and chena lands. Since the distribution of farm
size is fluid due to the continuous shift in chena cultivation, the present
and "future without project" conditions are derived by dividing total net
value of production by the estimated number of agricultural families in
the area.
5.        Under project conditions, farm size will be uniform at 2.5 ac but
most farmers will be either on upland soil (60% of the total) or lowland soils
(40% of the total). Different cropping patterns and cultivation methods will
be appliLed and hence two farm models are analyzed (Table 3).
6.        The following assumptions have been made in the analysis of the
farm budgets:
(a) Net Cultivable Land -- Under project conditions, the gross
size of each farm is 2.5 ac. Since a part of this area is
taken up by bunds, ditches, etc., the net cultivable area
is somewhat lower. Consequently, it is assumed that the
net cultivable land is 95% of the gross area.
(b) Crop Yields and Input Requirements -- Present and projected
yields used in the analysis are given in Annex 11. Crop
inputs have been estimated based on the input requirements
discussed above and given in Table 1.
(c) Cropping Patterns -- Lowland farmers would be double cropped
with a cropping intensity of about 185%. Due to the high
permeability of upland soils, a wide range of crops would be
grown; mostly with non-flood (upland) rice in the maha and
chillies, soybeans, groundnuts, cereals and pulses in the
yala. The cropping intensity would be about 170%.
(d) Prices and Subsidies -- The prices used in the analysis are
the financial farm gate prices discussed in detail in Annex
13, Appendix 1. Since two financial prices of various crops
are projected in the future, two farm budgets are estimated.
(e) Irrigation Cost -- At present, no water charges are levied for
tank irrigated paddy. After implementation of the project,
the total charges are assumed to amount to Rs 685 per farm
(para 14).
128



ANNEX 12
Page 4
(f) Homestead Income -- In addition to the ownership of paddy and
chena lands, most of the farmers also derive some income from
tree crops or cash crops grown in the homestead lots. The
average size for homestead lots under present and "future
without "project conditions is around 1.0 ac while it is
reduced to 0.5 ac under project conditions.
(g) Non-farm Income -- The current level is estimated from
the socio-economic survey of a similar area to the project
area around Rs 900 per year per family. The substantial
rise in agricultural income would increase demand for non-
farm services and hence increase employment opportunities
and wage rates. However, the labor requirements on the
family farm would also increase. Due to the lack of data
on consumption patterns and the supply of migrant labor,
it is assumed that the non-farm income will remain unchanged
in the future.
Distribution Effects of the Project
7.        Under the above assumptions, the net farm income, including the
imputed value of the farm produce consumed at home, is estimated to increase
from about Rs 3,200 per year at present to about Rs 8,560 for upland farms
and Rs 7,630 for lowland farms 1/ under "future with project" conditions.
Thus, as the result of the project, the farm incomes would be more than
doubled.
8.        For the new settlers who would be mostly landless laborers from
surrounding areas, the present income levels are unknown. However, they
are likely to be below those for the farmers currently in the project areas.
Furthermore, it is unlikely that without the project, their incomes would
experience any significant gains. Accordingly, the gains for the new set-
tlers would be even higher than those for the existing farmers.
9.        The landholding pattern under project conditions will be uniform.
As a result, it is expected that the income levels will be fairly equal. When
the production is valued at the projected GPS prices, the net incomes for
the lowland farms is around 23% higher than that of upland farms. On the
other hand, the net farm incomes of the upland farms are approximately 13%
higher than that of the lowland farms when the value of production is esti-
mated at the projected average market prices. In addition, since the crop-
ping pattern of the upland farm can be altered in response to the market
opportunities, the disparity between lowland and upland net farm incomes
would not be significant.
10.       In comparing the project area income level with the average national
income, the farm income figures presented above which include the imputed
value of home consumption valued at farm gate prices, have to be adjusted for
1/   At projected market prices.                                         129



ANNEX 12
Page 5
the difference between farm gate and local retail prices. Consequently, it
is estimated that the farm households have a net annual farm income of about
Rs 3,500 or a per capita annual income of Rs 620. This is 60% of the national
per capita income of about Rs 1,250 per year. In the future, the average
household income in the project area would increase from about Rs 7,600 to
Rs 9,700 per year or a per capita annual income of Rs 1,500 to Rs 1,900 per
year. Assuming that the national per capita income increases about 2% an-
nually, at full development, the project area incomes would be approximately
the same as the national level.
Cost Recovery
11.       At a discount rate of 10% per annum, the present value of the pro-
ject investments in irrigation infrastructure and land development are esti-
mated to be about Rs 180 million or about Rs 4,700 per ac. 1/ In addition,
the 0&M of the irrigation system would require an annual expenditure of about
Rs 80 per ac. Assuming the water charges were introduced after the construc-
tion oE the irrigation infrastructure, a full cost recovery would require a
total charge of Rs 5,250 per ac -- Rs 4,700 for recovery of capital costs and
Rs 550 for O&M costs or an annual charge of about Rs 740 per ac.
12.       The prime vehicle for recovery of capital costs for the project is
the Sale of State Lands Act under which developed land would be sold to the
settlers. The sales price would cover the cost of development below the turn
out and the amount of special assistance to the settlers. At present, the
price is estimated at Rs 8,880 per family (Rs 3,550 per ac of farm land),
which would be paid over 20 years at an interest of 4%. This gives an annual
charge of Rs 650 per farm in current terms. Assuming an expected rate of
inflation of 4.3% per annum (the average rate of inflation in Sri Lanka for
the last 20 years) and the settlement schedule in 1979 for the project area,
the present value of land payment is estimated at about Rs 1,245 per ac or
Rs 3,110 per farm.
13.       Operation and maintenance costs for the project would be recovered
through the Land Betterment Charges Act (LBCA). The LBCA would, inter alia,
enable the Government to impose water charges in all publicly-financed irri-
gation and drainage schemes taking into account factors such as amount and
dependability of water supply, increases in agricultural production, capital
and O&M costs. Detailed regulations for the actual implementation of the
land betterment charges in 1978 are under preparation. For the project, the
GOSL intends to charge Rs 30 per ac for the first three years after the
construction period and full O&M costs (Rs 80 per ac in current terms) for
the irrigation works in the following years. This would give a present value
of about Rs 495 per ac. The proposed level of charges (land payment and
water charges) would give a cost recovery index of 33%.
1/   Excluding sunk costs.
130



ANNEX 12
Page 6
14.       The price of land is based on the actual costs for development,
and it will be adjusted for inflation. However, once the land is sold to
the settler, the repayment will be fixed in current terms. For families
settled in 1979, it is expected that the annual repayment would amount to
Rs 740 per annum in current terms. Taking into account the expected infla-
tion, it is estimated that the land payment at full development (1986) will
amount to Rs 485 per farm in constant 1976 terms. The betterment charge, at
full development, is estimated at Rs 80 per ac or Rs 200 per farm in constant
1976 terms. These values have been entered in the farm budgets.
Farmer's Repayment Capacity
15.       Project Rent.  For the estimation of the farmer's ability and
willingness to pay water charges, it is necesary to estimate the "project
rent". In simple terms, project rent can be defined as the incremental net
income when all factors of production, except water and relatd services sup-
plied by the project, are given the rewards that are required for their co-
operation. Project rent is equal to the net incremental income less the
necessary rewards to family labor and management for their additional labor
and entrepreneurship.
16.       The calculation is complicated by the uncertainty about the yields
and the crop prices that the farmer can expect. One way of handling this
problem is by introducing explicit probability distributions and a function
that shows the "utility" or "value" of different income levels. Unfortunate-
ly, this would lead to severe estimation problems. A simpler approach is
adopted in this case. The risk aversion of the farmer can be taken into
account by using a certainty equivalent (CE) instead of the expected value
(EV). If the yields have a normal distribution which is known, CE can be
expressed as a function of EV:
CE = EV - n x s
where   s  = the standard deviation
n = a factor that expresses the
farmer's risk aversion.
17.       Some empirical studies indicate that the farmer's choice of crop-
ping patterns and production techniques can be accurately predicted for values
of 'n' in the interval 1 to 2 1/. The value of'n'decreases when the farm size
increases. Since the farm size is small and uniform under "future with pro-
ject" conditions and the farmers are relatively poor, under "future without
project" conditions, n = 2 is applied to all farmers.
1/   These studies, which have been carried out by the Development Research
Center and the South Asia Projects Department, are still unpublished.
131



ANNEX 12
Page 7
18.       The variation in historical yields in the project area is within
roughly 20% from the average values. This gives s = 0.2 x EV. Consequently,
CE = 0.6 x EV. However, under "with project" conditions, it is not likely
that two crops fail in the same year while under "without project" con-
ditions, there is basically one crop during the maha season. Thus, it is
assumed that CE = 0.6 x EV for the "without project" situation and CE =
0.8 x EV for the "with project" situation.
19.       The next problem which arises in the calculation of project rent is
the value of family labor. The overall labor demand would increase substan-
tially from .967 M man-days in the "future without project" situation to 3.9 M
man-days under project conditions. The market wage would move upwards and
family workers would require a somewhat increased return for their labor in
order to compensate for the lost leisure. The increase in average wage for
both hired and family labor with the project is derived from the "S - curve"
with the following assumptions:  (i) during the slack season when less than
50% of labor supply is employed, the wage rate is equal to Rs 5.0 per man-day,
(ii) during peak season when the available labor force is fully employed,
the wage rate is equal to Rs 8.0 per man-day, and (iii) when labor require-
ments exceed the labor supply in the project area, the wage rate is equal
to Rs 10.0 per man-day. This would give an average cost of labor at Rs
5.5 per man-day under the "future without project" situation and Rs 8.2 per
man-day under project conditions.
20.       The reward for management and entrepreneurship can, in part, be
related to the capital employed in the production if this is not included in
the crop production cost. But it is primarily a premium for the special
skills that farm management require. Furthermore, many activities are not
included in the crop labor requirements such as applying for credit, listen-
ing to extension officers, hiring and directing daily laborers, etc. Conse-
quently, it has been assumed that the "management fee" is equal to 10% of
the net returns.
21.       The derivation of project rent also depends on the assumptions for
crop prices. In Sri Lanka, two pricing systems are in operation: the Govern-
ment guaranteed price schemes (GPS) and the market prices. With the exception
of paddy, the free market prices often exceed the guaranteed prices. On the
other hand, the guaranteed prices would assure certain income level as opposed
to the widely fluctuated market prices in response to the change in production.
Due to the existence of this dual pricing system, four estimates of project
rent are derived in Table 4 based on four assumptions of prices:
I.   the present GPS prices;
II.   the prevailing average market prices;
132



ANNEX 12
Page 8
III.   the projected GPS prices; and
IV.   the projected average market prices. 1/
22.       Project rent varies from Rs 590 to Rs 4,725 per farm.  With the
proposed level of charges indicated above, the rent recovery index, at full
development, ranges from 14% to 114% for upland farms and 26% to 31% for low-
land farms. 2/ This shows that project rent is sensitive to the variations in
projected crop prices. Taking Alternative III (projected GPS prices) as the
basic case, the sensitivity of project rent to price assumption is tested by
shifting the guaranteed prices of paddy and accordingly the cost of labor in-
puts. 3/ This is depicted in the rent recovery index curves shown in Figure
1. For example, a 20% increase in crop prices would mean a 42% increase of
the project rent for upland farms and 36% for lowland farms. The potential
water charge would change from about Rs 550 per ac to Rs 780 per ac for upland
farms and from Rs 1,040 per ac tb Rs 1,400 per ac for lowland farms. Due to
the sensitivity of project rent to price level and the importance of the
Government guaranteed prices for food production in Sri Lanka, a decision
about the exact level of water charges would have to take into account the
expected change in Government agricultural pricing policy.
Critical Consumption Level
23.       The project area farmers and the new settlers are relatively poor
with present consumption level equal to about 40% of the national average
(Rs 1,400). 4/ At full development of the project, their incomes would be
brought up approximately to the national average. In order to estimate the
settlers' ability and willingness to pay water charges, a critical con-
sumption level (CCL) is estimated. Since the GOSL puts a heavy emphasis
on income distribution and the welfare of the lower income group, the CCL
is estimated to be in the range of two-thirds to three-fourths of the
average consumption level. 5/ With the present level of charges, at full
1/   For a detailed discussion on projected prices, see Annex 13, Appendix 1.
2/   Since the land/payment is fixed in current rupees, the rent recovery
index based on discounted present values is slightly higher (Table 5).
3/   Since the projected GPS prices of other crops than rice is based on the
typical ratio between their prices and that of rice, prices of other
crops also vary proportionally. The cost of labor inputs also varies
proportionally if it is assumed that the wage paid is equal to the value
of marginal product of labor. The costs of other inputs are held constant
due to the subsidy and tax structures associated with these inputs.
4/   Average per capita consumption in 1974.  Sri Lanka Country Economic
Memorandum, IBRD Report No. 1019a-CE.
5/   For a definition of CCL, see Central Projects Memorandum No. 8.4.
133



ANNEX 12
Page 9
development, the project area farmers would pay up to about 25%-35% of their
incomes over and above the CCL. Since the family incomes of the settlers
would be only at the same level as the average for the country, this level
of charges would be considered adequate.
24.       Four levels of water charges can be identified in Table 5.  The anal-
ysis indicates that full cost recovery would undoubtedly stretch the farmer's
ability to pay too far. Consequently, the farmer's incentive to use irrigation
water would be impaired. The present proposed level of charge is regarded as
appropriate for the project due to the following reasons:
(a) Income distribution -- the farms are small and even below
the critical consumption level. After full development,
the incomes of the settlers would not exceed the average
per capita income in Sri Lanka;
(1b) Incentives to the settlers -- increased foodgrain production
is an important national objective, the provision of irriga-
tion with associated settlement in the sparsely populated
dry zone is a prime means to achieve this objective. How-
ever, the settlers have to be given proper incentives to
buy the land and to fully develop their new farms;
(c) Government's pricing policies -- the present policy on
taxes and duties and the procurement prices for foodgrains
and other subsidiary crops are biased against the agricul-
tural sector. This imposes an implicit tax on the farmer; and
(d) Tradition -- Sri Lanka has not charged for irrigation water
in the past and there would be considerable opposition from
farmers if the charges are perceived as "too high".
