SRI LANKA
MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT II
Economic Analysis-/
General
1.        The major components of the project -- irrigation infrastructure,
on-farm development and production support -- are interdependent. The bene-
fits from one component depend on whether the others are implemented. Thus,
they will be evaluated as a package. However, in order to justify the com-
position of the package, benefits and costs associated with each of the
components will also be analyzed separately.
2.        The normal approach for analyzing irrigation projects assumes that
the opportunity cost of water is zero. This assumption cannot be made in
the estimation of the benefits resulting from the on-farm investment and land
preparation by farm tractors which would lead to substantial water savings.
Consequently, the value of water is taken into account in the economic
analysis of these two components.
3.        The economic analysis of the project basically follows standard
Bank methodology. However, instead of using a shadow exchange rate (SER)
to translate foreign exchange costs and earnings into local market prices,
a standard conversion factor (SCF) has been applied to domestic market prices
in order to translate them into border prices. This will not affect the eco-
nomic rate of return of the project since the SCF is directly related to the
SER and the official exchange rate (OER):  OER/SER = SCF.
Basic Assumptions
4.        Standard Conversion Factor (SCF).  An SCF = 0.6 is applied to the
domestic component of the project costs and benefits in the economic analysis.
The sensitivity of the economic viability of the project to this assumption
is tested by using a 20% error in the estimated SCF. The detailed calculation
of the SCF is discussed in Appendix 1.
5.        Savings.  Following Bank standard practice, no premium has been put
on public or private savings. Consequently, public income and private con-
sumption are assumed to be equally valuable.
1/   This Annex was prepared before March 1977 re-evaluation.  If it is
assumed that the re-evaluation will not lead to the introduction of
either higher import duties or quantitative restrictions, the basic
rate of return would fall from 21% to 19%. However, it is more likely
that more quantitative restrictions would be introduced. In this case,
the economic rate of return would remain basically unchanged.
14 1



ANNEX 13
Page 2
6.        Agricultural Inputs and Outputs.  The economic prices for these
goods are discussed in Appendix 1.
7.        Construction Costs.  The construction of roads, irrigation and on-
farm Lnfrastructure is carried out by heavy equipment, and skilled and un-
skilled labor. For the economic analysis, the conversion factor of each
method is estimated as follows:
(a) Capital-intensive methods: The economic cost for capital-
intensive works is calculated directly from the estimated
input and output factors of machines used in the construction.
The procedure for this calculation presented in Table 1 shows
the build up of the economic cost for ripping operation by a
180-200 hp crawler tractor. The conversion factor for this
operation is 1.10. Similar exercises which are applied to
other types of capital-intensive works give conversion factors
ranging from 1.0 to 1.2. Therefore, the conversion factor
1.10 is applied to all types of capital-intensive works. 1/
(b) Skilled Labor. The skilled labor component is adjusted with
the standard conversion factor of 0.6 to get the economic
costs.
(c) Unskilled Labor. The opportunity cost of unskilled labor is
derived in Appendix 2. This gives a shadow wage rate of
unskilled construction labor at two-thirds of the market
wage rate. Since the unskilled labor component is expressed
in domestic price, a standard conversion factor of 0.6 is
applied for the economic analysis. A summary of the con-
version factors for the construction costs is given below.
Conversion Factor
Capital-Intensive Works                          1.10
Skilled Labor                                    0.6
Unskilled Labor                                  0.4
Construction Costs (weighted average)            0.75
1/   The financial bid prices for the civil works are not translated to
the economic prices by applying the standard conversion factor due to
the following reasons:
(i) the competitive situation for construction companies in Sri
Lanka is such that the price is set at the break-even point.
(Where it is equal to the lowest average variable cost.)
(ii) there are a large amount of indirect taxes in the construction
companies--bid prices which have to be excluded in the economic
analysis.
142



ANNEX 13
Page 3
8.        Sunk Costs.  Most of the investments under the Stage I project,
which diverted Mahaweli water from Polgolla to the Stage II area, were pre-
requisited for the proposed project. Stage I created the potential for
subsequent investments, which would have shorter gestation periods and give
relatively high returns. The sunk costs for the Polgolla diversion do not
affect the economic viability of the proposed project. However, in order to
establish whether the initial decision to provide irrigation to the Stage II
area was sound or questionable, the sunk costs have to be included in the
analysis. The expenditures involved in Stage I are adjusted to the amount of
water allocated to the present project area to derive the sunk cost per acre
for the Stage II area. Detailed analysis is provided in Table 2.
9.        Value of Water.  On-farm development and land preparation by farm
tractors would result in substantial water savings. This water can be pro-
ductively used for increased cultivation. Consequently, if the water is
used for increasing cropping intensities, the opportunity cost of water
saved is estimated. Under project conditions, the average field water re-
quirements are estimated to be about 6,500 m /ac per annum. The net value
of production is estimated at about Rs 2,300 per ac at full development.
This gives an opportunity cost of water about Rs 0.35 per cubic meter.
Various studies on the impact of irrigation on the increase in foodgrains
production also give a value of water in the range of Rs 0.19-0.38 per
cubic meter. 1/ Conservatively, a value of Rs 0.3 per cubic meter of
water at the field outlet is applied in the economic benefits of land
development and farm power investments.
10.       The opportunity cost of water varies depending on the areas
where the water saved is applied. For the project area, where there are
many control structures, the conveyance efficiency is higher than that of
other areas. Consequently, the marginal productivity of water in the
project area is valued at Rs 0.3 per cubic meter whereas the marginal
productivity of water in other areas is valued at Rs. 0.2. 2/
1/   The World Bank's appraisal report on the Andhra Pradesh Irrigation
and Command Area Development Composite Project calculates the oppor-
tunity cost of water about Rs 0.20-0.30 at the field outlet with
the command area development program. An IRRI paper on A Field
Comparision of Rotational and Continuous Irrigation in the Uppoer
Pampanga River Project indicates that for the padddy yield level
of 3.25-3.50 tons per hectare, the application of one cubic meter
of water would result in an increase in production in the range
of 0.12-0.24 kg. At the economic price of paddy Rs 1,600 per ton,
the value of water is about Rs 0.19-0.38 per cubic meter.
2/  Assume conveyance efficiency is 0.5 for other areas compared to
0.75 for the project area.
143



ANNEX 13
Page 4
11.       Based on hydrological studies, it has been estimated that about
30% of water losses are regained further down in the system (Annex 2). Of
the water saved after taking the charge into account, it is assumed that
half would be used for irrigation within the project area and half outside
the project area. This gives an average value of Rs 0.175 per cubic meter.
On Farm Development
12.       On-farm development would result in both higher yields and
increased irrigation efficiencies in the project area. The higher yields
would be a direct benefit to the participating farmers whereas higher
irrigation efficiencies would lead to increased agricultural production
in other areas. To which farmer and to which area the benefits would
accrue would depend on water allocation decisions. If each area were to
be given a fixed water allocation, the higher irrigation efficiency would
result in either a higher cropping intensity or planting of more water
consuming crops. The water saved could, alternatively, be used to irrigate
other areas, thereby benefiting a different group of farmers.
13.       On-Farm Benefits.  Since a major part of the lowland farms in
the project area have been leveled by the farmers, the benefits of a careful
on-farm development program would mainly accrue to the upland crops. It
is estimated that the yields of these irrigated crops would increase by
15% to 25%. At full development, this would mean an increase in benefits
on the order of Rs 400-600 per ac. Land leveling would also result in a
yield decrease as it disturbs the thin layer of top soil. Various
studies 1/ indicate the loss of soil fertility resulting in yield
decreases from 20% to 50% depending on the method of land leveling (i.e.
manually or by bull dozer). Since most of the land leveling and shaping
in the project area is done manually, it is assumed that the yield would
decrease about 20% and 5% in the first and second year, respectively,
if nc additional fertilizer is applied to compensate for the loss of
soil fertility. The following pattern of benefit build-up is assumed
for the on-farm development.
Year            0      1      2      3        4       5       6-10
Benefits %      0     -67   +17   +67      +83      100       100
14.       External Benefits.  On-farm development would result in a sub-
stantial saving of water which could be used to increase cropping intensity
withiLn the project area and to irrigate other areas. The net irrigation
water requirements for the various upland crops in the project area is
estimated at about 2,200 m  per ac per year.  Without on-farm development,
1/   A Field Comparison of Rotational and Continuous Irrigation in the
Upper Papanga River Project, IRRI mimeograph, undated.
144



ANNEX 13
Page 5
the field efficiency of upland farms would be around 0.45 whereas it would in-
crease to 0.6 with on-farm develop3ent.  Thus, the investment would result
in a water saving of about 1,200 m per ac. This gives an annual benefit
of about Rs 210 per ac per annum.
15.       A summary of economic costs and benefits is shown in Table 3.  The
economic rate of return is estimated at about 26%.
Farm Tractors
16.       Land preparation by farm tractors would result in labor and water
savings in the project area. It is estimated that land preparation by
manual labor would require about 20 man-days per ac more during maha and
15 man-days more during yala than that by bullock or by tractors. In addi-
tion, since the soils in most of the dry zone areas at the beginning of the
maha and yala cultivation seasons are extremely baked and difficult to
break, cultivation by buffalo or manual labor is either not possible or
would require a substantial amount of water to soften the soils. Conse-
quently, mechanical cultivation would lead to a substantial savings of
water which could, alternatively, be used to increase cropping intensity
within the project area or benefit a different group of farmers in other
areas.
17.       Labor Saving.  As land preparation is normally carried out in
the months of August, September for the maha and January, February for
the yala, the requirement of labor without tractors for this purpose will
exceed the peak monthly labor supply in the project area. The economic
opportunity cost of labor without tractors is then estimated by using the
"S-shaped" curve, described in detail in Appendix 2. This gives Rs 5.5
per man-day. Since land preparation by manual labor would require 20 man-
days per ac in maha and 15 man-days in yala, the cost of this operation
would amount to about Rs 190 per year per ac. The economic cost of land
preparation with a four-wheel tractor is estimated at about 29.00 per hour
for 3.3 hours of primary and secondary tilling per ac (about Rs 94 per ac
per annum).
18.       Water Saving.  The gross field 3irrigation requirements for
various crops is estimated about 6,500 m per ac per season. Since land
preparation with tractors would shorten the duration of the operation and
reduce water requirements to soften the soils, the amount of water saved
could be significant.  Conservatively, it is assumed that 10% of the gjoss
water requirements are saved. This gives water savings of about 650 m per
ac. The benefits of water saved would amount to about Rs 115 per ac. Given
these assumptions, the economic rate of return is estimated at about 20%
(Table 4).
145



ANNEX 13
Page 6
Extension Services
19.       Two major factors determine the benefits of extension: (i) the aver-
age increase for farmers who follow extension advice and (ii) the number of
farmers who adopt improved techniques. The proposed extension programs in
Sri Lanka would have similar characteristics as the programs that have recently
been introduced in Bank Group-assisted projects in India. The experience so
far has indicated that after two years some 20% to 30% of the farmers are
followers and that their yields have increased some 10% to 20%. The impact
of the extension program is greater in areas which are covered by on-farm
development. Consequently, it has been assumed that about 30% of the farmers
within the project area would be followers (of which 20% are contact farmers)
with yield increases of some 20% to 30%. On the average, this would increase
the value of production by Rs 120-180 per ac per annum compared to the annual
economic cost for extension services of Rs 24 per ac. Consequently, the bene-
fit cost ratio for the extension program is highly favorable.
Marketing, Storage and Processing
20.       The provision of marketing, storage and processing facilities in
the project area would result in savings from handling and storage losses
and transportation costs as well as improvement in quality of rice to the
constumers. The benefits derived from these components have been quantified
as far as possible to be included in the economic analysis of the basic case.
It is conservatively estimated that the inclusion of these components would
reduce the losses in handling, storage and transport from 7% in future with-
out the project to 5% in future with the project.
Social Infrastructure
21.       The economic costs of social infrastructure are excluded from the
basic case of the analysis since the benefits derived from them are basically
unquantifiable. However, as the failure of many settlement schemes in Sri
Lanka shows, these components of the project would be important for the
success of the settlement program, and thus, help to achieve the projected
production increase. For this reason, an economic rate of return for the
project, including the costs for social infrastructure, has been estimated.
However, since most of the benefits of these components are unquantified,
this gives a conservative estimate of the rate of return for the whole proj-
ect package.
Investment Package for the Main Project Area
22.       Economic Rates of Return (ERR).  The main project area would receive
a full package of project components. The economic viability of the invest-
ments in this area is also an indication of the viability of the whole Stage
II dLevelopment. The benefits and costs for the basic case 1/ (excluding
1/   See paragraphs 4 to 8.
146



ANNEX 13
Page 7
agricultural research and social infrastructure) for the assumed 50 year life
of the project are shown in Table 5. The economic rates of return for the
investments in the main project area are estimated at: 1/
(a)  Basic case                    21.2%
(b)  Basic case plus sunk costs   14.4%
(c) Basic case, including sunk
costs plus agricultural
research and social
infrastructure                13.6%
23.       Sensitivity Analyses.  Several tests were made to determine the
sensitivity of the rate of return estimates to various alternative assump-
tions. The results which are summarized in Table 6, show that the project
is moderately sensitive to changes in the standard conversion factor and the
shadow price of labor.
24.       Project Risk.  The project would provide sufficient water for
the proposed cropping pattern. Thus, the risk associated with water supply
is limited. The following tests illustrate the risk associated with other
factors:

ect should be justified on the basis of the viability of the whole
Stage II development. Since each of the areas would receive the same
package as the main project area, the viability of the Stage II devel-

the main project area.

SRI LANKA
MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT  II
Prices for Economic and Financial Analyses
General
1.        The estimated future economic prices of internationally traded
agricultural inputs and outputs are derived from the projected 1985 world
market prices expressed in 1976 currency values with appropriate adjust-
ments for freight, handling and processing. Since pulses and chillies
are not traded internationally in any appreciable quantity, the Bank Group
does not put out price projections for them. The economic prices for these
two crops are then based on the long-term average ratio between their
cif Colombo prices and world market price for rice. 1/
2.        The GOSL operates a guaranteed price scheme for most major crops
in order to encourage production. However, with the exception of paddy,
the free market prices often substantially exceed the Government guaranteed
prices. For the period 1959-1976, the free market price of paddy differed,
on the average less than 5% from the Government guaranteed prices. Typically,
half of the marketed surplus of paddy is sold to the PMB, and consequently the
average farm gate price differs less than 3% from the guaranteed price. For
farm budget analyses, it is assumed that the farm gate price of rice is equal
to the guaranteed price for rice at the 1976 level in constant terms. Two pro-
jected financial prices for other crops are estimates. One is based on the
long-term average ratio of the guaranteed prices of these crops and that of
rice; the other is derived from the typical ratio of their market prices
and the guaranteed prices for rice.
Official Exchange Rate (OER)
3.        Until May 1976, the Rupee was officially valued at a Pound Sterling
rate of US$1 = Rs 15.60. Since then it has been fixed against a "basket" of
currencies. As these currencies are floating, the US Dollar/Rupee exchange
rate is subject to change. Conversions of financial prices have been made
at US$1 to Rs 8.60 which was the short-term average rate prevailing at the
time oE appraisal.
1/   The ratio between rice cif Colombo to rice fob Bangkok (5% broken)
is about 1.03.
156



ANNEX 13
Appendix 1
Page 2
4.        In order to promote "non-traditional" exports and to cut-down
on non-essential imports, the Government practices a dual exchange rate
system through the sale and purchase of Foreign Exchange Entitlement
Certificates (FEECs). At present, about 35% of current receipts and 61%
of current payment of foreign exchange are subject to the FEEC rate of
about US$1 = Rs 14.20 (FEEC = 165% of OER).
Standard Conversion Factor (CF) - Shadow Exchange Rate (SER)
5.        In order to take into account the level of protection provided
by tariffs and trade restrictions, an SCF is applied to the price of non-
traded goods and to consumption. In the absence of trade restrictions,
the SCF can be approximately calculated through the formula:
SCF =X+M
X + S  -T  + FEEC  + M + TM + FEECm
where X  = fob value of exports at OER
M  = cif value of imports at OER
S  = export subsidies
T  = export taxes
TM = import duties
FEEC = FEECs on exports
FEEC = FEECs on imports
6.        For 1975, this calculation gives SCF = 0.75.  Since this estimate
does not take into consideration the quantitative restrictions, 1/ it pro-
vides only an upper limit for the SCF. The unofficial market rate of Rs 15
to 20 2/ gives a lower estimate of about 0.50 for SCF but this estimate
partially reflects the demand for luxury imports and for capital flight.
7.        Absence of any economy-wide models and the presence of a complicated
system of import restrictions, taxes and duties makes it difficult to estimate
a correct value of SCF. For the purpose of economic analyses, an SCF of
0.60 has been used. This gives a SER approximately equal to the FEEC rate. 3/
1/   Since 1970, imports on subsidiary crops such as chillies and pulses
and vegetables were banned.
2/   Mission's estimate.
3/   The SCF is directly related to the SER and the OER:  SCF = OER
157



ANNEX 13
Appendix 1
Page 3
In order to test the sensitivity of the economic viability of the project
to this assumption, the analyses have been done assuming a 20% error in
the estimated SER. The resulting SERs and SCFs are:
US$1 = Rs
Official Exchange Rate                              8.6
FEEC Rate                                          14.2
SER:  Base Case (SCF = 0.60)                       14.3
SER:  Alternative 1 (SCF = 0.50)                   17.2
SER:  Alternative 2 (SCF = 0.75)                   11.5
Prices of Agricultural Outputs
8.        The economic prices for rice, cereals, pulses, and chillies are
based on their border prices at the official exchange rate.  The domestic
cost components (i.e. local transport and marketing charges) are adjusted
by the standard conversion factor (SCF) of 0.6.
9.        The economic prices for non-traded outputs such as sesamum and
vegetables are derived by multiplying the economic prices of paddy to the
ratio between the financial prices for these crops and the price for paddy.
Prices of Agricultural Inputs
10.       Fertilizers.  Fertilizers in Sri Lanka have been sold with a 50%
subsidy since the late 1960s except for a few months in 1974 when the subsidy
was eLimanated inspite of record high world market prices. The ratio between
fertilizer price and paddy price during the 1970s has been favorable (an
average of 0.75). Even during the short period of non-subsidy, the price
ratio was around 1.5. Consequently, as the projected world market prices
for fertilizer have declined substantially from their 1974 levels, they
are thus projected to remain well below those levels or substantially reduced.
The present farm budgets are calculated with the prevailing rate of subsidy
while the future farm budgets are based on the assumption that all subsidies
would be removed.
Diesel Fuel
11.       The price of petroleum is expected to remain on its present level
of about US$11.6 per barrel fob Ras Tanura (Saudi Arabia). The prevailing
financial price of Diesel Fuel Rs 5.30/gallon is assumed for the farm budget
analysis and an economic price of Rs 4.3/gallon when taxes are deducted and
the domestic cost components are adjusted with a standard conversion factor
(SCF) of 0.6.
158



ANNEX 13
Appendix I
Page 4
Land Preparation
12.       Much of the land preparation work in the project area is done
on a custom basis either by bullocks or tractors. The prevailing rental
charge for tractors is Rs 190/ac and for a pair of buffaloes four bushels
of paddy per ac, excluding driver. 1/ The cost of land preparation by
bullocks is around Rs 212 (the cost of paddy is Rs 33 per bushel plus
the wage rate of farm labor estimated at ten man-days per ac for Rs 8 per
day). However, since there is a credit element in the rental charges for
bullocks, it is assumed that the average financial prices for land pre-
paration is Rs 190 per ac whether it is done by animal or farm power. For
economic analysis, the estimates of rental charges per ac for four-wheel
tractors, two-wheel tractors and bullocks are derived in Appendix 2. Since
the economic cost of land preparation by each method is roughtly equal, it
is assumed that the economic cost is Rs 100/ac(Table 2).
Agricultural Labor
13.       The peak wage rate for farm labor is around Rs 8 per day for men
and Rs 6 per day for women and children. During the slack period (Yala
season), the wage rate is around Rs 5 per day for man and Rs 3 for woman
and children. The economic cost of labor is based on a seasonally adjusted
opportunity cost in terms of output forgone in alternative employment. The
details of the analyis are given in Appendix 2.
1/   Mission's interview with the farmer.

SRI LANKA
MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT II
Farm Labor Analysis 1/
General
1.        The settlers in the project area are predominantly engaged in
subsistence farming. It consists of paddy cultivation under village tank
schemes, chena cultivation on upland soils and planting tree and cash crops
on the homestead lots. The cultivated area per family is small and the
average farm income low. Consequently, temporary work on the non-farm sector
has become one of the major sources of providing income. Little employment
data exist for the projet area but some rough estimates can be presented based
on the socio-economic surveys (1975-75) by the MDB as well as the 1971 census
of the whole island.
2.        Because of relatively high underemployment, current wage levels
(reported in the project area) problably overstated the economic opportunity
cost of farm labor. For the economic analysis, the opportunity cost of labor
has been derived from the "S-shaped" curve (shown in Figure 1). The assump-
tions behind the curve are discussed below.
Labor Supply
3.             Population.  Based on prelimninary results from the 1976 socio-
economic survey carried by the MDB, it is estimated that there are around 6,900
families living in the area, of which 10% are engaged in non-agricultural activ-
ities. As the project is implemented the landholding pattern is recognized and
additionmal families will be brought into the project area. Under "without
project" conditions, it is assumed that the existing population wil lgrow at a
rate of 2% per annum. 2/
4.        In Sri Lanka, women from low income families do work in the fields
but the participation rate is lower than that of men. Based on the results of
the Land and Labor Utilization Survey in 1975 by the Central Bank of Ceylon,
1/   The analysis is restricted to the main project area (H4 and H5).  Since
the employment situation and cropping patterns are similar for all other
areas, the qualitative results apply to the whole project area.
2/   The average growth rate for the population of the whole island from
1963-74.
163



ANNEX 13
Appendix 2
Page 2
the labor force participation rate for females is approximately 20%, whereas
it is 54% for males in the rural area, or an average of 37%. Since labor
requirements for the project area are estimated in terms of man-day equi-
valent, the labor force participation rate for the project area is adjusted
downward to 33%.
5.        Farm Labor Supply.  In the present situation, around 10% of the
existing families in the project area are engaged in non-agricultural activ-
ities such as trade and commerce, Government services and construction. Based
on the experience in Hl area, it is expected that the existing non-agricul-
tural faAilies will be accommodated in the new scheme to provide services for
the new settlers.  In addition, the increase in total population in the proj-
ect area will also increase the demand for non-agricultural activities such as
education and health, trade and commerce. Thus it is assumed that the pro-
portion of non-agricultural families will remain constant in the future.
6.        In the project area, some members of the existing agricultural
families are also engaged in permanent non-agricultural activities. The re-
sults from the socio-economic surveys indicate that roughly 10% of the work-
ing members of agricultural households are permanently employed in non-farm
sectors. Under "without project" and "with project" conditions, it is also
assumed that 10% of total labor force from the farm families will be perman-
ently employed outside agriculture as the result of the increase in the de-
mand for non-agricultural services. The estimates of farm labor supply per
annum given below, have been made using 250 days per year. This allows for
less than a full day's work for the young and the old who are included in
the labor force estimate and for people working long hours during the peak
months of agricultural activities (the peak monthly labor supply is higher
than the average for the year).

Labor Demand
7.        Farm Labor.  Estimates of monthly farm labor requirements for
various crops are given in Table 1. Estimates of total monthly demand are
given in Table 2. In total, the project would increase farm employment
from about 0.74 M man-days in future "without project" to 3.91 M man-days
per annum. The increase in demand for labor arises from the significant
increase in cropped areas as well as more intensive land use.
8.        Non-Farm Employment.  The project would offer scope for a substan-
tial rise in agricultural income and hence generate additional income and
employment in the non-farm sector. The lack of reliable data on non-farm
employment at the present situation and data on consumption patterns in the
project area makes it extremely difficult to project accurately the employ-
ment effect in the future. However, experience from similiar areas in
Asia indicates that an increase in agricultural production of Rs 15-20,000
would generate one man-year of employment in the local non-farm sector. 1/
As a result of the project, the gross value of agricultural production
would increase by some Rs 150 M, the increase in non-farm employument would
be on the order of 9,000 man-years, of which about 8,000 man-years would be
attributed to the increased production in the main project area (H4 and H5).
9.        Homestead Employment.  As mentioned in Annex 12, the farmers in
the project area also derive some income from tree crops or cash crops grown
in the homestead lots. Based on the results from the socio economic survey
of the project area, it is estimated that labor requirements for homestead
farming are 30 man-days per family under present and future project "without
conditions" and 15 man-days in future "with project".
Economic Opportunity Cost of Farm Labor
10.       Scarcity of detailed data on farm income levels, off-farm employ-
ment opportunities and the agricultural labor force profile makes the evalu-
ation of economic shadow rates for farm labor extremely subjective. Economic
farm labor costs were estimated by using monthly shadow wage rates reflecting
different opportunity costs associated with various segments of the labor
force and slack and peak periods during the year.
11.       On the basis of avalilable information from the project area and
experience from similar areas, it is postulated that the marginal opportunity
cost of farm labor can be represented by an "S-shaped" curve. The marginal op-
portunity cost of farm labor is positive at all levels of demand and increases
1/ Draft Report on Rural Non-Farm Employment and Earning Opportunities
in Rural Areas and Town, Development Economics Department.
165



ANNEX 13
Appendix 2
Page 4
when more labor is absorbed into field work. The increase in the opoportun-
ity cost is initially slow and reflects the scarcity of alternative pro-
ductive employment, but it is more rapid when full labor supply is utilitized.
At employment level above the maximum available in the area, the opportunity
cost would again increase at a slower rate since a large number of migrants
would be attached to the project area from neighboring areas where there is
substantial underemployment. In practice, it is reasonable to approximate
this idealized "S-shaped" curve by three straight segments.
12.       Figure 1 gives the curve of approximate shadow rates used in the
analysis. The curve shows the estimated marginal opportunity cost of labor
as a function of demand (expressed as a proportion of the supply). The shape
of the curve is based on the following assumptions.
(a) at minimum employment levels, the marginal cost would be
the value of additional consumption needed for family
members to undertake field work (Rs 1.5 per man-day 1/)
plus the value of foregone activities such as fishing, home
improvements, etc., (Rs 0.5 per man-day);
(b)  when the monthly labor employment just exceeds the off-
peak family labor supply (taken as 50% of the full supply),
the marginal cost would be the slack market wage rate
(Rs 5.00 per man-day 2/);
(c) at a monthly labor demand corresponding to the present
peak level, the marginal cost would be equal to the maximum
market rate (Rs 8.00 per man-day);
(d) when the employment is equal to or larger than the
estimated supply of farm labor from the area, the labor
would be drawn from the non-farm activities and opportunity
cost would increase to about Rs 10 per man-day, since at
this wage, a large number of migrants would be attracted
to the project area.
13.       The economic opportunity cost of farm labor, expressed in border
prices, is equal to the area under the curve to the employment level for
each month multiplied by the SCF. The increased monthly labor cost due to
the project is shown in Table 2.
1/   Taken as 75% of the current average daily consumption of foodgrains.
2/   Farm family members of the labor force not regularly employed else-
where during slack periods.
166

Appendix 2
Page 5
14.       The total labor requirement will increased from .97 M man-days
with the project to 3.91 M man-days with the project. The economic cost
for this increase is Rs 8.98 M which gives an implicit cost of Rs 3.00 per
man-day.

ANNEX 13
Appendix 3
SRI LANKA
MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT II
Construction Labor Analysis
General
1.        The construction of irrigation infrastructures such as distributaris,
field channels and minor drains in the project area has been traditionally
carried out by the labor intensive method. In addition, a large part of
jungle clearing and on-farm works such as land levelling and construction of
bunds are also expected to be contracted to local labor.
2.        The main source of supply of construction labor comes from the
settlers in and around the project area whose employment opportunities
are restricted to chena cultivation and temporary work in non-farm sector.
With the exception for the maha season, the unemployment and underemployment
rates are high. Consequently, the traditional method of valuing construction
labor at the market wage rate would overstage its economic opportunity cost.
Hence, for the economic analysis, shadow wage rate for construction labor
is derived.
Economic Opportunity Cost of Construction Labor
3.        The output foregone in alternative employment of the settlers in
the project area is represented by the net income from chena and homestead
cultivations and from employment in non-farm sector. Since chena cultivation
consists of cereals, chillies, pulses in the maha and sesamum in the yala,
it is assumed that the cropping pattern for the upland crops in the project
area is also applied for chena crops. The average chena acreage for families
who do not have any paddy land is 1.6 ac. The income from chena cultivation
is approximately Rs 1,120 per family. Homestead cultivation yields a net
income approximately Rs 200 a year and non-farm employment Rs 900 per annum.
4.        The estimated average labor supply per family is based on a labor
force participation rate of 33% and assuming 250 working days per year. The
financial opportunity cost of construction labor (Rs 5.0 per man-day) is
estimLated to be about two-thirds of the wage rate for construction labor in
the project area (Rs 7.50 per man-day). The economic cost per man-day,
expressed in border prices, is derived by adjusting the financial opportunity
cost by the SCF of 0.6.
